BURMA
NYAUNGGYAT DAM MULTIPURPOSE PROJECT
Staff Appraisal Report
I. Sectoral Background
General Economy
1.01      Burma is potentially one of the richest countries in South Asia.
Apart from impressive natural resources, it is endowed with a healthy and
highly literate population and a relatively low population density (120/sq mi).
It is an agriculture-based country with 77% of its 32 M inhabitants living
in rural areas and dependent directly or indirectly on agriculture. Burma's
260,000 sq mi are made up of four climatic and topographical regions: the
Western and Northern Hills, Shan Plateau, Central Region, and Arakan and
Tenasserim Coastal Regions. About 27% of the land (45 M ac) is considered
arable, but only about 20 M ac are currently cultivated since rainfall and
access to water supplies are limited in the remaining cultivable areas.
Forests containing several species of valuable timber, particularly teak,
cover almost half of Burma, while swamps, rivers, lakes, settlements, and
grazing land comprise about one quarter of the country.
1.02      Despite its considerable natural resource base, favorable man-land
ratio, and high degree of literacy, Burma's economic record historically has
been disappointing. With per capita GDP of about US$150 p.a., it ranks among
the 25 least developed nations. The guiding philosophy of the present Govern-
ment (GOB) is based on a combination of traditional Burmese values and socialist
doctrine that attempts to reorient the economy toward adequate physical neces-
sities and to improve social services. Although it has made some progress
towards social goals (land redistribution, education, health care), GOB's
ability to raise the material well-being of the population has. been constrained
by slow economic growth. The increase in value added by the directly productive
sectors during the 1960s and early 1970s scarcely matched population growth
(2.2% p.a.), while export volumes declined for most of the period. Low export
earnings in turn reduced the economy's capacity to import raw materials, spare
parts, and capital equipment, leading to further underutilization of capacity
in most sectors and severely limiting expansion.
1.03      The critical question, therefore, is the quality with which Burma
can manage and develop its human and natural resources. To improve economic
performance, GOB began over the 1971-78 1/ period to institute basic reforms
such as selective increases in agricultural procurement prices, currency
devaluation, increased interest rates on savings deposit, tax reform, reorga-
nization of the banking system, managerial and organizational reform of State
1/   Dates expressed are in terms of Burmese fiscal years, April 1-March 31.



- 2 -
Economic Enterprises, and import liberalization. As a result of these reforms
and generally favorable climatic conditions, growth accelerated under the
Second Four-Year Plan (SFYP) (1975-78). Gross domestic product (GDP) increased
by an annual average of 4.8% in real terms, compared with 2.7% during the
previous decade. Agricultural growth recovered after 1975 thereby bolstering
the export and manufacturing sectors. Gross capital formation practically
doubled over the four-year period, and domestic savings expanded. Exports
grew by 10.6% p.a. in current terms (1975-78) principally reflecting export
price increases and expanded rice exports.
1.04      Burma's recent economic performance provides a relatively firm
base to intensify development efforts under the Third Four-Year Plan (TFYP)
(1979-82). Ambitious targets have been set for overall GDP growth (6.6% p.a.)
and individual sectors, notably agriculture (5.8%), industry (12.2%), and
power (14.3%). In support of these targets, total investment would accelerate
to levels (11.2% p.a.) more than double rates achieved under SFYP, and public
sector investment would increase by over 150% of the level realized during
SFYP. While planned increases in investment and growth are desirable, these
must be complemented by major improvements in absorptive capacity and by
continued reforms begun during the 1970s.
The Agricultural Sector
A.   Role of Agriculture in the Economy
1.05      The agricultural sector, including livestock, forestry, and fisheries,
accounted for 36% of 1978 GDP in current prices and employed 68% of the labor
force. Agricultural products, primarily rice and teak, represent about 85% of
all exports. More than 60% of Burma's industrial output stems from the process-
ing of agricultural crops including paddy, cotton, jute, and sugarcane. Paddy
is by far the most important crop, accounting for about 42% of the value added
for agricultural crops and more than half of the sown area. Other major crops
in terms of sown area include sesame, pulses, groundnut, maize, cotton, sugar-
cane, wheat, chillies, jute, tobacco, onions, coconut, and rubber. The agri-
cultural sector performed poorly over the decade prior to 1976; its average
annual growth rate (1.6%) was less than both the 2.3% growth in GDP and 2.2%
in population. During that decade, exports in constant prices decreased by
about two-thirds largely due to declining rice exports. The poor performance
of the agricultural sector was a major cause of Burma's slow economic growth
during this period. Its origins can be traced to insufficient public and
private investment in agriculture, inadequate production incentives, and
shortages of inputs and agricultural support services.
1.06      There has been considerable improvement in the sector's performance
since 1976 with an annual growth rate averaging about 3.8% (1975-78), reflecting
more favorable weather conditions, higher world timber prices, and GOB action
to redress past problems. In re-ordering its investment priorities to give
more emphasis to agriculture and other primary producing sectors, GOB has
increased agriculture's share of State capital expenditure from 11% for the
1964--74 period to about 20% during SFYP and to a targeted 27% for TFYP.  Over



- 3 -
the past few years, GOB has also raised procurement prices for several impor-
tant crops, notably jute, cotton, and sugarcane, and has attempted to improve
input availability and agricultural support services.
B.   Principal Project Area Crops
1.07      Paddy.  Paddy, Burma's main crop, accounts for about 55% of the
total sown area and is the country's main export commodity. Production has
grown slowly during the last decade, increasing by only 2.0% p.a. Once the
largest rice exporter in the world, Burma suffered considerable damage to
embankment structures during World War II and subsequently from recurrent
social disturbances and inadequate embankment maintenance. Over the last
15 years, there has been no significant increase in sown area nationwide as
increases in acreage in Upper Burma have merely compensated for losses in
delta areas of Lower Burma. Adoption of high-yielding varieties (HYVs) has
been constrained in the past by the lack of material inputs (especially seeds
and fertilizers), unavailability of locally adapted varieties, inadequate
water control, and draft power shortages during peak demand periods. As a
consequence, yields stagnated up to 1976, and paddy production fluctuated
around pre-war levels of 8 M tons. However, because of favorable weather
and increased input availability, paddy yields increased from 1,570 lbs/ac
in 1975 to an estimated 1,840 lbs/ac in 1979, and total production expanded
from 8.4 M to 10.3 M tons over the period, or by an average of 5.2% p.a.
Under TFYP, Burma hopes to boost paddy production to 11.0 M tons by 1982, or
by an average of 4.2% p.a., principally through greater adoption of HYV pack-
ages. While open market paddy prices currently offer growers considerable
incentive to increase paddy production, particularly in rice-deficit areas,
the GOB-regulated compulsory paddy procurement system and its prices, which
have been maintained low and essentially unchanged since 1974, dampen some
of this incentive. Sensitive to the inflationary and budgetary consequences
that might spring from modification of this important national system, GOB
has attempted to mitigate this constraint to growth by instituting over the
last several years a series of input supply incentives complemented by insti-
tutional and technical support--a program that includes substantial subsidies
on critical inputs (paras 8.07-8.12).
1.08      Cotton.  Faced with growing demand for cotton textiles and inade-
quate supply of raw materials to service its manufacturing capacity, Burma
has been forced to import increasing quantities of cotton yarns, lint, and
fabrics. Cotton cultivation was introduced into Burma in the late 1950s
to supply its newly installed cotton manufacturing industry.  To ensure a
reliable supply, cultivation within the irrigated areas of Upper Burma's dry
zone was advocated and bolstered by initially favorable compulsory procurement
prices. The cotton development program proved unsuccessful, however, basic-
ally because of unadjusted and increasingly non-competitive prices from 1961
to 1971, mandatory cropping in unsuitable areas and season, and unsatisfactory
varieties. As a result, sown area declined from 616,000 in 1965 to 362,000 ac
in 1970, and total seed cotton production dropped from 68,000 to 34,000 tons
over the five-year period. Sown area has fluctuated widely over the past
decade reaching a high of 552,000 ac in 1972 and low of 402,000 ac in 1977;
since then area sown to cotton increased to 461,000 ac in 1979. During most
of the last decade, seed cotton production stagnated around 40,000 tons, but



- 4 -
increased to 52,000 tons in 1979. Part of the problem historically has been
the wide divergence between sown and harvested areas particularly reflecting
the fact that about 90% of the cotton crop is grown under rainfed conditions.
Also, despite GOB attempts to reduce competition with other crops by mandating
use of irrigation water for cotton and by doubling procurement prices in
January 1974, farmers have neglected compulsory cotton plantings and concen-
trated efforts on more lucrative crop alternatives.
1.09      With a doubling of the cotton procurement price again in March-
December 1976, yields have reflected farmers' increased incentives: the
national average yield rose from 220 lbs of seed cotton per ac (1971-77)
to 290 lbs in 1978-79. Higher yields from new varieties, better extension
effort, more assured water supplies, and concentration of cotton on more
suitable soils and in a less risky season, as proposed in the project, should
further stimulate production. Reflecting these reorientations, TFYP estab-
lishes a rather ambitious 1982 target of 64,000 tons of medium-staple cotton
production, requiring an average annual growth of 14.7% over 1978 levels.
C.   Performance and Problems
1.10      To reinforce and accelerate recently achieved economic growth, pro-
gress must be made in rationalizing the role of agricultural and industrial
prices in the allocation of resources. At present, investment and production
decisions are guided by plan targets rather than changes in production and
market conditions. Apart from rigidity in controlled pricing systems, the
problems confronting agriculture include insufficient supplies of essential
farm inputs, inadequate transportation facilities, and shortages of farm power
and basic farm services, in particular agricultural credit and extension. If
Burma's agricultural performance is to be improved, intensive and coordinated
action will be necessary to deal with these requisites.
The Power Sector
A.   General
1.11      Electric power supply in Burma is governed by the Electricity Supply
Act of 1948. Under this act, GOB nationalized the electric energy supply
industry and formed the Electricity Supply Board. The Board was later reor-
ganized into the Electric Power Corporation (EPC) under the Ministry of
Industry. Since then the Ministry of Industry has been split into the Ministry
of Industries I and II. EPC is now one of seven statutory corporations under
the Ministry of Industry II (MI-2) that includes other corporations relating
to heavy industry and petroleum. EPC is mandated to supply the electric power
requirements of government institutions and the public; to be responsible for
the installation of new generating plants, transmission lines, substations, and
distribution systems; to formulate and implement schemes to develop the system;
and to conduct business in a manner similar to that of a commercial enterprise.
EPC basically has autonomy in its daily operations; however, responsibility
for decision-making in important matters such as system planning and invest-
ment rests with MI-2.
1.12      In 1978, average consumption per capita in Burma was estimated
at about 26 kWh and average generation per capita at about 34 kWh, including



- 5 -
that provided by captive generating plants owned by GOB departments and indus-
tries. The latter figure compares with 15 kWh in Nepal, 24 kWh in Bangladesh,
and 156 kWh in India.
1.13      At the end of 1978, about 469,000 consumers were connected to the
system, mostly in the major cities. About 7% of the population had access
to electricity. Currently, there is a waiting list of over 150,000 potential
consumers who cannot be served, mainly because of inadequate distribution
facilities.
B.   Energy Resources
1.14      Available energy resources in Burma include hydro potential, petro-
leum, and gas, of which the most abundant is hydro potential. Theoretical
hydro potential was estimated at 100 M kW and 370 billion kWh p.a. in a power
survey carried out under the UNDP/IDA Burma Umbrella Project (January 1977 to
June 1978). It appears that economically exploitable hydro resources in Burma
would be sufficient to meet power demand for the foreseeable future, although
considerable time will be needed to develop these resources. Various pre-
feasibility studies indicate that about 20 sites might be suitable for hydro-
electric exploitation, totalling more than 5 M kW and 20 billion kWh of annual
energy.
1.15      Present crude oil production in Burma is about 30,000 barrels per
day (about 10 M barrels p.a.). Detailed information on total oil reserves
is not available, but proven reserves are estimated at about 100 M barrels.
This suggests that it would not be prudent to rely on this scarce resource
for long-term power generation.
1.16      Natural gas fields are principally located along the Irrawaddy River
in Central Burma. Reserves have been variously estimated at 150 billion cu ft
(on a proven basis) 1/ to 462 billion cu ft 2/. While detailed information on
total gas reserves is not available, even with the latter estimate, reserves
would provide only about a 22-year supply for two existing fertilizer plants
(Sale and Kyunchaung), two existing gas turbine stations (103 MW), a committed
fertilizer plant (Pyalo), and two committed gas turbine stations (72 MW).
This limitation suggests that it would not be desirable to commit further gas
resources for power generation.
Government Development Policy and IDA Lending Strategy
1.17      With the issuance of TFYP, GOB reaffirmed its commitment to
(a) higher and sustained growth supported by increased investment, (b) con-
tinued priority for agricultural development as the principal mechanism for
generating and conserving foreign exchange, (c) increased labor productivity
and employment, and (d) greater, but judicious, utilization of the country's
natural resources to facilitate growth. IDA's lending strategy has supported
1/   From the Oil and Gas Journal, December 1978.
2/   From the Power Survey under UNDP/IBRD Umbrella Project (June 1978).



- 6 -
these GOB objectives by aiming to ease the most immediate constraints to growth
and, particularly, at increasing and diversifying production for export to
alleviate the shortage of foreign exchange. Within this overall strategy,
primary emphasis is being given to the development of agriculture, forestry,
and mining. Within this framework and where possible, priority is being given
to quick-yielding projects. At the same time, infrastructure-oriented projects
are proposed and are being financed in such areas as transportation, telecom-
munications, and power.
1.18      Since 1973 IDA has made 14 credits to Burma totalling US$272.3 M
(net of cancellations). About 58% of this assistance was channelled to the
agricultural sector, consisting of eight projects: irrigation, rubber,
livestock, seeds development, two paddyland development projects, and two
forestry projects. About 15% of IDA's assistance has supported the rehabili-
tation of the transport sector through three projects, namely inland water
transport, railways, and ports development. The remaining IDA assistance
has funded projects in telecommunications (21%) and mining (6%). Projects
in various stages of preparation involve the following sectors: forestry,
telecommunications, fisheries, and power. The proposed power project would
finance installation of transmission lines to integrate committed gas turbine
power stations.
1.19      In the past, lending was partly constrained by limited knowledge of
key sectors and the lack of adequately prepared projects. Ongoing projects
and sector work have improved the information base and a UNDP-financed, IDA-
executed Technical Assistance Project (US$4.1 M) has developed a project pipe-
line in critical sectors such as agriculture, transport, power, and industry.
Technical assistance packages financed through project lending have also
helped improve Burmese project preparation capabilities, as well as adminis-
trative and managerial skills, through in-service and overseas training.
1.20      The proposed Nyaunggyat project would mark the largest Burmese
project ever financed by IDA, and the proposed IDA credit would represent
the largest for Burma to date. While involving a relatively long implementa-
tion period, its objectives are in consonance with GOB's development goals,
including the development of hydro potential wherever feasible, and with IDA's
strategy of supporting sound growth-oriented investments. The project would
include agricultural supporting services and would complement ongoing or pro-
posed projects geared to alleviating technical and institutional constraints.
II. THE PROJECT AREA
Location
2.01      The project area is located in Kyaukse, Myittha, Wundwin, and Tada-U
Townships in Burma's central dry zone. The main highway from Rangoon to Mandalay
passes through this area, which is situated about 40 mi south of the city of
Mandalay. The project area extends 48 mi north to south and 20 mi from east
to west. It stretches from the foothills of the Shan Plateau almost to the
Irrawaddy River, 30 mi away to the west (Map IBRD 14543).



7-
Climate
2.02      The project area, has a tropical monsoon climate with three distinct
seasons: a rainy season from mid-May to mid-October, a dry cool season from
mid-October to mid-February, and a hot dry season from mid-February to mid-
May. The rainfall gradient is sharp, with much more rain at and near the dam
site than in the command area out on the plain. Average rainfall in the com-
mand area is only about 32 inches p.a., with one peak in May-June and another
in September-October. Temperature variation at Mandalay ranges during the year
from a January average of 70 F to a May average of 87 0F.  However, minimum
temperatures can fall below 500F, and maximum temperatures can rise above 104 0F.
Humidity varies with temperature and rainfall, and leads to considerable evapo-
transpiration in the hot dry months from March to May (Annex 1, Table 4).
Topography and Soils
2.03      About 80% of the currently irrigated area to be rehabilitated
through the project consists of heavy soils of fine texture, much of which
have imperfect drainage. The remaining soils in this area are of coarse tex-
ture and are largely light and moderately well-drained. In the new area to
be irrigated through the project, about 15% of the area contains heavy soils
of fine texture while the remaining soils are largely light and moderately
well-drained. The soils in both the currently irrigated area and the dryland
tract range from slightly acidic to slightly alkaline with pH ranging from
6.6 to 8.3.
2.04      Based on topography, soil texture, and drainage conditions, land
in the project area is broadly grouped into two categories: paddyland and ya
land. Generally, paddyland is in flat, low lying areas with heavy soils, and
ya land is on upland with well-drained medium or light soils. The project's
net command area comprises 85,400 ac of paddyland and 116,100 ac of ya land.
Most of the paddyland (80%) is in the currently irrigated area while most of
the ya land (83%) is unirrigated at present. Further land development statis-
tics are given in Annex 1, Table 1.
Water Resources and Drainage
2.05      The existing irrigation system depends for its water supply on the
unregulated natural run-off of the rivers. The catchment area of the Panlaung
River at the Kinda Weir site measures about 970 sq mi. Annual streamflow
(1950-77) averaged about 1,765 cusecs at Kinda Weir, which fluctuates depend-
ing on precipitation in the upper basin area. Minimum streamflow usually
occurs in March-April, sometimes reduced to less than 70 cusecs. The main
natural drains in the currently irrigated area are the Panlaung, Samon,
Htongyi, Dan Chaung, and Pauk Chaung Rivers. All drains have large cross-
sections, particularly in flatter areas, but have been partially eroded by
flows and provide relatively poor drainage. There are also some man-made
ditches providing only limited drainage. Additional drainage for the area
is considered essential.



-8-
Irrigation Facilities
2.06      The existing Panlaung irrigation system commands, by means of three
diversion weirs (Kinda, Nathwle, and Kyime) on the Panlaung River, a gross
area of 117,000 ac. The total net acreage nominally supported by irrigation
facilities is 94,000 ac, but due to land abandonment from flooding and drain-
age hazards, only about 88,000 ac are actually supported. Currently, only
partial irrigation can be provided for this area because of heavy silting in
existing canals.
2.07      Conveyance systems are designed for proportional distribution with-
in subsystems serving net areas of about 3,000 to 25,000 ac and have a duty of
one cusec per 50 ac to meet requirements of monsoon paddy. The feeder canal,
main canals, distributaries, and minors are all unlined. The sizes of water-
course areas vary between 50 and 200 ac each, with an average of 178 ac, and
the length averages about 7,000 ft. These watercourses directly serve about
25% of the bunded plots or basins having an average size of one ac. The re-
maining area, consisting mainly of bunded paddy fields, is supplied indirectly
from field to field. Basins are flat and generally well-levelled. For dry
season crops, basins are sometimes furrowed.
Floods and Flood Control
2.08      About 10,000 ac of the rehabilitation area have been affected
annually by flooding over the 1968-75 period. The Panlaung River is the
principal source of flooding, especially between the Nathwle and Kyime Weirs
where the bankful capacity (3,000 cusecs) is inadequate to control even small
floods. The river spills onto the adjacent land, which lies 8-9 ft below bank
level at certain locations. Between Nathwle and Kyime, low sections are pro-
vided with a 2-5 ft high embankment. There is only one flood escape structure
located on the left bank upstream from the Kyime Weir, and its escape capacity
is inadequate.
Farm Size, Land Tenure, and Population
2.09      Farm Size.  About 14,400 farm families, each averaging six persons,
occupy the 88,000 ac in the currently irrigated tract, while about 8,990 farm
families till the 102,500 ac 1/ of land in the currently dryland area. Aver-
age farm sizes therefore range from about 6 ac in the irrigated area to 11 ac
in the dryland area, compared with the national average of 5.4 ac. A more
detailed breakdown of farm areas and the number of farmers by farm size in-
terval is given in Annex 1, Tables 2 and 3.
2.10      Land Tenure.  In Burma, all land belongs to the State.  Cultivators
have been regarded as tenants of GOB since the enactment of the Tenancy Act
of 1964.  They cannot transfer land, and their heirs cannot possess land as a
matter of right. When a farmer dies, the Village Land Committee (VLC) may
distribute his land to another cultivator if the deceased leaves behind only
1/   Total new development area is 113,500 ac, of which 11,000 ac would be
cleared and developed with the project (para 5.14).



- 9 -
minor sons or if his sons are in non-farming occupations. VLC may even take
away all or part of a farmer's land under the Land Nationalization Act of 1953
for failing to grow prescribed crops. Land ownership in this context means
only the right of usufruct. The pattern of ownership is highly skewed in
the project area, as it is in all of Burma. In the currently irrigated area,
for example, 40% of farms are less than 4 ac in size but occupy only 17% of
total farmland. On the other hand, farms over 10 ac represent 20% of all
farms but occupy 48% of total farmland.
2.11      Population.  The population within the project area is estimated
to be about 200,000 distributed within the four townships. There are about
33,000 rural families, which include 23,000 farm families and 10,000 landless
families, the latter providing most of the necessary input of hired labor on
farms. At peak periods of agricultural activity, however, migrant labor comes
from the poor agricultural areas of neighboring Meiktila and Myingyan Townships.
This seasonal migration is expected to increase when the demand for hired labor
increases with pvoject development. Population data by tract appears in
Annex 1, Table 1.
Agricultural Practices, Yields, and Production
2.12      The currently irrigated tract in the project area is one of the few
places in the central dry zone under intensive cultivation. Cropping intensity
ranges from about 90 to 190% depending on the availability of irrigation water
in the dry season. Average intensity ranges from about 90% on partially irri-
gated ya land to 140% on paddyland, giving a weighted average of 125% for the
entire irrigated area. Paddy is the predominant crop (58%), followed by medium-
staple cotton (27%), sesame (15%), chillies (11%), pulses (8%), and onions
(6%). Present cropping patterns in this area are determined not only by soils,
availability of irrigation water, drainage, and prevailing market prices, but
also by special GOB programs. GOB now requires that priority in irrigation
water use be given to cotton in the dry season, and that farmers devote about
30% of farm area to this crop during this season.
2.13      Since the proposed new area is not currently served by any irriga-
tion system, crop production is entirely dependent on low and variable rain-
fall. Overall cropping intensity is only about 72% as large areas of culti-
vated land must at times be left fallow for lack of adequate soil moisture at
sowing time. Pulses are the principal crop (26%), followed by oilseeds (23%),
chillies (9%), short-staple cotton (8%), and paddy (6%). Details of project
area cropping patterns are given in Annex 1, Table 8.
2.14      Paddy grown is mostly indica-type, local varieties.  HYVs account
for only about 18% of area sown to paddy, mainly C4-63 and some IR-5. IR-20
and IR-22 varieties are just now being introduced. Because of water con-
straints in the dry season, paddy is cultivated at present chiefly in the
monsoon period. Monsoon paddy is transplanted in July-August and harvested
in December-January. Pre-monsoon HYV paddy, accounting for only 3% of paddy
acreage, is sown in April/May and harvested in July-August. With year-
round irrigation it would be possible to grow two crops of paddy (para 8.02).



- 10 -
2.15      Burma grows both short- and medium-staple cotton, all in the central
dry zone. Short-staple cottons are Gossypium Arboreum varieties (Mahlaing 5/6,
Wagyi, and Wagale) and medium-staple cottons, Gossypium Hirsutum varieties,
which include Stoneville 7 and Stoneville 213. During the 1978 season, the
project area contributed about 15% of the national crop estimated at 41,700
tons. Short-staple varieties are grown in the mid- to late-monsoon seasons on
upland areas as a rainfed crop, while medium-staple varieties are grown with
basin irrigation on flat land with heavy soils more suited to paddy culture
as a pre-monsoon crop. The present cropping season for medium-staple cotton
is unusual and inappropriate in that the crop is sown in February-March and
harvested in July-August during the mid-monsoon period. Irrigation water is
often in short supply during the growing season while later, heavy rains
coupled with poor drainage adversely affect yields as well as seed and lint
quality. Attempts are now being made to grow medium-staple crop as a late
monsoon crop on well-drained, irrigated ya land in Kyaukse Township adjacent
to the project area; results obtained at the region's Lungyaw cotton seed
farm are encouraging (Annex 1, Table 12).
2.16      Present crop yields are low.  Yields in the irrigated area average
only about 1,500 lbs per ac, local paddy; 1,900 lbs, HYV paddy; 500 lbs,
medium-staple cotton; 500 lbs, chillies; 170 lbs, sesame; 300 lbs, pulses;
and 3,600 lbs, onions. Low yields are primarily attributable to poor seeds,
unreliable supply of irrigation water, and absent or inadequate application
of fertilizers and pesticides. In the case of medium-staple cotton, an addi-
tional reason for low yields is the improper cropping season (para 2.15).
2.17      Present agricultural production from the project area comprises
about 38,800 tons paddy, 6,100 tons cotton, 3,400 tons chillies, 2,000 tons
sesame, 700 tons groundnut, 3,600 tons pulses, and 8,800 tons onions.
Agricultural Supporting Services
2.18      The Agriculture Corporation (AC) is responsible for agricultural
research and extension, procurement and supply of fertilizers and pesticides,
and land use investigation and records. The Agricultural Mechanization
Department (AMD) is responsible for providing custom tractor services at
subsidized rates, with priority given for cotton cultivation.
2.19      Research.  Basic research is done at the Agricultural Research
Institute (ARI) at Yezin, 100 mi south of the project area. ARI currently
is assisted by UNDP (equipment and staff) and by the IDA-financed Seeds
Project (land development) in research on training, soils, and crops (paddy,
pulses, and cotton). Applied research is carried out at the 16 central exper-
imental stations throughout the country. The Mandalay station specializes in
research on basin-irrigated cultivation of rice, chillies, oilseeds, pulses,
and onions while that at Mahlaing (about 70 mi southwest of the project area)
concentrates on short-staple cotton.   In addition, the Hlaingdet cotton farm
(70 mi southeast of the project area) conducts research on medium-staple cotton
under rainfed conditions.
2.20      ARI and the above-mentioned experimental farms would support the
project through relevant research efforts and provision of foundation seed.



- 11 -
However, since furrow irrigation is not yet practiced in Burma and since the
existing rainfed cotton research farms (Mahlaing, Hlaingdet, and Lungyaw) are
not typical of the ya land within the project area, it would be essential to
establish a new research farm stressing furrow irrigation for medium-staple
cotton and other upland crops for the newly irrigated area. This new farm
would also serve as a demonstration and training center (see para 5.22).
2.21      Agricultural Extension.  AC fields about 96 extension agents within
the four project townships, or on average about one agent per 600 farmers.
Through the years the extension service has proven ineffective because:
(a)  AC is required to give priority to the formulation and
implementation of annual regional and village agricultural
plans, encompassing production and input supply, rather
than farm-level extension;
(b)  AC's extension methods have become obsolete and over-
extended;
(c)  technical advice given to farmers often lags behind latest
research findings;
(d)  AC staff is handicapped by lack of transport and instruc-
tion materials; and
(e)  extension workers are frequently diverted to non-agricultural
work (e.g. accompanying township council members on various
administrative tours).
Recently AC has taken steps to strengthen its extension program including
progressive in-service training for extension workers and introduction of
the "Training and Visit" (T&V) system. The Ministry of Agriculture and
Forests intends to replicate T&V started under the Lower Burma Paddyland
Development I and II Projects (Cr. 642-BA and 835-BA) in the four project
townships. AC has also recently been relieved of responsibilities for cotton
and jute procurement thereby freeing manpower to concentrate on production-
oriented activities such as extension.
2.22      Inputs.  There are two AC seed farms in the project area, one for
paddy at Kyaukse and one for medium-staple cotton at Lungyaw. Paddy seeds
produced at the Kyaukse seed farm are multiplied by selected seed growers;
AC buys back seed produced at a 10% premium over the paddy procurement price.
Procured seeds are then sold by AC to farmers at cost. No meaningful quality
control is practiced, however, and seed is generally indistinguishable from
commercial grain. The quality of cotton seed is worse since there is no
properly organized cotton seed multiplication program. The Seeds Develop-
ment Project (Cr. 745-BA) would improve and expand seed industry development
through modest paddy and cotton seed multiplication programs. The Lyngyaw
seed farm would be one of six farms developed under the Seeds Project.
2.23      Although AC generally allocates fertilizers for application only
on HYV paddy and medium-staple cotton in the project area, farmers also use



- 12 -
fertilizers on attractive cash crops such as chillies, sesame, and onions.
Present application rates on HYV paddy and cotton are much below recommended
dosages. Controlled supplies, currently sold at subsidized prices (para 8.09),
are often insufficient to meet demand. Use of pesticides is also very limited
mainly because of inadequate sprayers and a basic lack of farmer confidence in
the efficacy of AC-supplied pesticides.
2.24      Farm Power Support.  Land preparation for crops other than cotton is
usually done by bullocks, while that for cotton is increasingly done by four-
wheel tractors, the services of which are generally hired from AMD at highly
subsidized rates (Annex 3, Table 14). GOB mandates that AMD give priority to
cotton. AMD has a medium-sized workshop at Myittha for repair of assemblies,
but this station is not as yet fully equipped. The tractor stations at Kyaukse
and Wundwin, which are maintained in temporary structures, have only hand
tools to service equipment.
2.25      Agricultural Credit.  The Myanma Agricultural Bank (MAB) was estab-
lished in 1976 to reactivate provision of seasonal, medium-, and long-term
credit to farmers through the existing Township Banks (TBs) and Village Banks
(VBs), as was the case prior to 1974. With the termination of the advance
purchase system for paddy in 1978, seasonal credit to paddy farmers is now
provided by MAB through TBs and VBs with assistance from the Township and Vil-
lage People's Councils. This change was chiefly responsible for the seven-
fold increase in MAB loans made in 1979 (K 822.3 M) over 1978 levels. Recovery
is made with the help of the above councils and the Agriculture and Farm
Produce Trade Corporation (AFPTC), responsible for compulsory procurement
collections; the loan recovery rate historically has been good (averaging
93.2%, 1976-78). The current rate of K 70 per ac of paddy is considered
inadequate. MAB has recently taken the initiative to provide seasonal loans
for paddy at enhanced rates varying from K 100/ac for ordinary varieties to
K 280/ac for special HYVs for farmers participating in the IDA-financed Lower
Burma Paddy Development Projects and the ADB-financed Sedawgyi Project. Also,
as MAB formulates a medium- and long-term lending program through technical
assistance financed by the Overseas Development Administration (ODA) of the
UK, it would start lending for land clearing, equipment such as power tillers
and sprayers, cattle, and other capital investments.
2.26      Farmers receive interest-free advance purchase payments for cotton
at the rate of K 210/ac from the Textile Corporation (TexCorp) under the
Ministry of Industries I (MI-1). Advance purchases for other crops (sesame,
groundnuts, and chillies), at about 55% of the estimated production value, are
provided by farmer cooperatives. MAB also provides seasonal credit for cash
crops (other than cotton); but for these crops, farmers have generally relied
on their own resources.
Marketing and Processing
2.27      Farmers are required to sell specified minimum quantities of paddy
to AFPTC according to a progressive quota system and may sell any remainder
on the open market usually only within their own townships. Paddy procured
by AFPTC is milled at Kume, Myittha, Wundwin, and Kyaukse which have a total
milling capacity of about 50,000 tons p.a. At present, however, only about



- 13 -
one-third of this capacity is used (para 8.13). Quantities of paddy reserved
for on-farm consumption is generally processed at home or at small artisanal
village mills. Most medium-staple cotton produced is sold to TexCorp. Seed
cotton purchased is ginned and baled, and the lint is supplied to TexCorp
mills. Ginning is performed near Kyaukse and Myittha, and all baling at
Kyaukse. With the exception of one roller ginning unit established recently,
practically all ginning facilities in use are antiquated and were originally
installed to process short-staple cotton. The marketing system for the cash
crops such as chillies, oilseeds, and onions is private and well-organized.
Transportation
2.28      The main highway from Rangoon to Mandalay passes through the Kume
within the project area. Myittha is on a road that branches off from this
highway and goes west to Myingyan. A road from Kyaukse to Tada-U skirts the
northern boundary of the project area. An unpaved road along canal banks
connects the Lungyaw farm to the main highway. Busses travel frequently along
the main highway, providing transportation to farmers with small amounts of
vegetables (chillies, onions, tomatoes) for marketing in Kyaukse and Mandalay.
Larger quantities of vegetables, paddy, cotton, and sesame are trucked to
Mandalay. Trucks, both private and state-owned, also travel to Myingyan,
Meiktila, and Rangoon. The Rangoon-Mandalay railway line passes through
Kyaukse, thus also providing railway linkage with the project area.
III. POWER SYSTEM, SALES, AND DEMAND FORECAST
Existing Facilities
A.   Generation
3.01      The Electric Power Corporation (EPC) is principally responsible for
the generation and distribution of power in Burma, although some industries
and institutions have small power plants for their own use. EPC has in serv-
ice a total of 390 MW of generating capacity, or 86% of the total generating
capacity in Burma. EPC's total generation in 1978 was 931 GWh, of which 72%
was supplied by the 168 MW Lawpita Hydro Power Station--the only major hydro
power station in the country--located about 220 mi northeast of Rangoon.
There are four thermal power stations, namely Kyunchaung Gas Turbine (54 MW),
Myanaung Gas Turbine (49 MW), Ahlone Steam (35 MW), and Ywama Steam (30 MW),
which respectively provided 14%, 3%, 3%, and 0.5% of EPC's total generation
in 1978. The remaining 7.5% was supplied by 50-1,000 kW diesel generators
scattered throughout the country with a total capacity of 54 MW (Map IBRD
14546).
B.   Transmission and Distribution
3.02      A 230 kV transmission line (250 mi) between Lawpita station and
Rangoon and a 132 kV transmission line (225 mi) between Lawpita station and



- 14 -
Mandalay form the hydro-grid system that integrates Lawpita, Ahlone, and
Ywaman power stations and five major substations having a total capacity of
244 MVA. The Kyunchaung gas turbine station, which operates on non-associated
gas and is located about 100 mi southwest of Mandalay, is connected with this
hydro-grid system at Chauk, but is operated electrically independent due to
the sensitivity of the Sale fertilizer plant to fluctuations in voltage.
3.03      Myanaung gas turbine station, which is located about 120 mi north-
west of Rangoon and also operates mainly on non-associated gas, supplies
electricity to Prome and the northern part of the Irrawaddy Division through
a 66 kV transmission line (60 mi). An additional 66 kV transmission line,
financed by ADB, from Myanaung station to Myaungmya (160 mi) has recently been
completed, supplying electricity to almost half of the Irrawaddy Division from
Myanaung.
3.04      The distribution system consists of about 730 mi of 33 kV primary
distribution line and 6,800 mi of various secondary distribution lines at 11,
6.6, 3.3, and 0.4 kV. Losses in the EPC system, including station use, have
decreased from 29% in 1970 to 24% in 1978. The latter figure compares with
33% in Nepal (Nepal Electricity Corporation) and 37% in Bangladesh (Bangladesh
Power Development Board). According to EPC's loss analysis for 1977, the
breakdown of losses was 2.1% for station use, 6.7% for transmission, and 16.4%
for distribution. High distribution losses are mainly due to overloading in
distribution systems in Rangoon and Mandalay. EPC has obtained a US$20 M
suppliers' credit from Yugoslavia to augment its distribution systems and
plans to reduce total system losses to 20% by 1986.
Energy Sales and Consumption
3.05      EPC's annual generation and energy sales since 1970 are shown in
Annex 1, Table 13. EPC's sales increased at an average annual growth rate of
10.3% during the period 1970-78. The average growth rate was 9.3% p.a. during
the first 4-1/2 years, and increased to 11.8% during the last three years of
SFYP. The Burmese economy as a whole is expected to achieve at least as high
a growth rate during the next few years under TFYP (1979-82). Sales by con-
sumer category are noted in Annex 1, Table 14.
3.06      The past growth rate of the largest energy sales category, namely
industrial consumption, increased to 15.2% p.a. during the 1975-78 period
compared with 12.7% in 1970-75. The share of industrial sales increased from
46% in 1970 to 57% in 1978, indicating the country's progress in industrial-
ization. TFYP proposes even faster industrialization, targeting 12.2% annual
average growth for the industrial sector as compared with its past record of
7%, in 1975-78.
3.07      Domestic electricity consumption increased 10.1% p.a. during the
last three years compared with an annual growth rate of 6.5% during the pre-
vious 4-1/2 years. However, consumption per customer still remains low at
about 450 kWh p.a. The share of domestic sales decreased from 32% in 1970
to 27% in 1978. Other usage (commercial, street lighting, etc.) shared only
16% of total sales in 1978.



- 15 -
Demand Forecast
3.08      EPC's demand forecast, covering the period up to 1991, provides
for an average annual growth rate of 10.8% in total sales and 10.5% in system
demand over the 1978-91 period. This forecast is based on category-wise pro-
jections of energy requirements, taking into account the historical growth
rate for each consumer category and superimposing the energy requirements of
new large industries that are expected to materialize within the next five
years.
3.09      Projected growth rates in sales by each consumer category until 1986,
when the proposed Nyaunggyat project comes on stream, are summarized below:
Annual Growth Rate in Sales
(in percent)
(Actual)    --------(Forecast)------
Sales Category                 1975-78      1978-86            1978-91
Domestic                          10.1         10.4              10.4
Industrial                       15.2          15.6              12.3
Large Industries              22.0         19.5                 -
Small & Medium Industries    7.3             7.0                -
Other Usage                        4.3          1.6               1.7
Total Sales                 11.8         12.7               10.8
3.10      The average annual increase in energy sales is projected at 14.2%
to 1984 compared with 11.8% over the past three years.  Beyond 1984, EPC has
conservatively projected an increase in demand of about 8% p.a. since little
reliable information is available especially concerning industrial demand (70%
of projected total sales in 1984). Consequently, the average annual projected
increase to 1986 would be 12.7% p.a.
3.11      Based on projected energy sales, the system's peak load was esti-
mated by taking into account expected improvement in losses and load factors.
The loss factor is expected to improve from the present 24% to 20% by 1986
and the load factor from 63% at present to 65% by 1982. Demand forecasts to
1991 are shown in Annex 1, Table 15.
Current Development Program
3.12      The current development program for the EPC system is derived, in
part, from recommendations of a power sector survey carried out in 1978 under
the UNDP/IDA Burma Umbrella Project, designed to forecast a long-term power
demand and to establish a sound development plan for the power sector. The
current development program for new generating plants is shown in Annex 1,
Table 17. GOB has committed itself to installing six 18 MW gas turbines: two
in Mann for operation mainly on associated gas, two in Prome for operation on
associated and non-associated gas, and two in Rangoon for operation on heavy
residual oil. Additionally, GOB has recently obtained financing from ADB to
construct the Sedawgyi hydroelectric plant (two 12.5 MW turbines), which will



- 16 -
come on stream in 1984. The development program currently includes three
additional power plants: Chauk (18 MW gas-fired thermal), Yenangyaung (18 MW
gas-fired thermal), and Zangtu (24 MW hydro). The two thermal power plants
would substitute for the capacity of Ahlone (30 MW), which is currently
planned for retirement by 1982. GOB also plans to connect the two new thermal
power plants (Chauk and Yenangyaung) with the committed Mann and Prome gas
turbine plants by a 132/230 kV transmission line. All of these projects are
planned for completion before the proposed Nyaunggyat power plant.
3.13      A full appraisal of EPC and the power sector was not expected to be
carried out. Hence, confirmation was not obtained that EPC's current develop-
ment program is in fact the least cost long-term expansion plan. Questions
affecting such a confirmation would include: verification that economic and
sufficient gas is available at Chauk and Yenangyaung for the generation pro-
posed; whether the Ahlone thermal plant should be retired in the near future
or retained for reserve; and a load flow analysis to establish the need for
adequate transmission capacity between Rangoon and Chauk (para 5.35).
Need for Additional Power Projects
3.14      The peak demand/generating capacity balance in the hydro-grid system
(Annex 1, Table 17) indicates that existing reserve capacity plus additional
capacity obtained from committed generating plants will be fully utilized by
1985. As far as the peak demand/generating capacity balance is concerned, it
was assumed that new capacity in Chauk and Yenangyaung would replace that
retired at Ahlone. To meet demand growth after 1985, GOB plans to construct
the Zangtu hydro plant (24 MW), the study of which, however, is still in an
early stage. Even if the Zangtu plant were completed, based on demand fore-
casts, the system would still require additional capacity in 1986. Although
the power survey under the UNDP/IDA Burma Umbrella Project identified the
Paunglaung hydroelectric project (136 MW), the earliest feasible completion
date for this project would be about 1988, considering the time needed for
preparation of detailed designs and financing arrangements. Since there are
no other hydro schemes ready for implementation, the projected power demand
could be met by either the proposed Nyaunggyat project or a fossil fuel
thermal alternative (paras 9.07 and 9.08).
Need for Extended Transmission System
3.15      Considering its geographic location, the proposed Nyaunggyat plant
would supply electric power mainly to the Mandalay, Thazi, and Chauk substa-
tions. Existing transformer capacities in these three substations are 36 MVA,
15 MVA, and 40 MVA, respectively. The Thazi substation is already fully
loaded, and projected demand will exceed the transformer capacity of the
Mandalay substation before the Nyaunggyat plant comes on stream. These two
substations, therefore, need extension (para 5.36).
3.16      EPC's future development program includes a Thazi-Toungoo trans-
mission line in 1985. This transmission line is designed to transmit power
north to south and to increase system reliability, thereby reducing the
vulnerability of the Lawpita-Toungoo and Lawpita-Thazi transmission lines.
Considering the future development of the Paunglaung hydroelectric project



- 17 -
as well as system stability, this transmission line should be a 230 kV line
instead of 132 kV, as currently planned (para 5.36).
IV. PROJECT INVESTIGATIONS AND SUITABILITY
Investigations and Project Formulation
4.01      Engineering investigations of the Nyaunggyat dam site to develop
water storage along Panlaung River were first undertaken during the 1967-71
period by the Irrigation Department (ID), assisted by the Soviet Consulting
Engineers of Salkhozpromexport, USSR. These investigations resulted in the
1971 report, "Nyaunggyat Reservoir Project," leading to a request in 1973 for
IDA assistance in financing the project. In 1975, Engineering Consultants,
Inc. was commissioned to prepare a feasibility report with subsequent appraisal
by IDA in May-June 1976. During field appraisal the mission concluded that
the geological soundness of the dam site was questionable and that a more
detailed investigation of the upper and lower dam sites was necessary for
sound development.
4.02      Further engineering studies of the Nyaunggyat Dam, financed from
IDA Cr. 483, were carried out by Lahmeyer International (LI) of the Federal
Republic of Germany (FRG), engaged by GOB in 1977 to investigate both the
upper and lower dam sites and to conduct a feasibility study for the more
suitable site. The consultant's detailed investigations and studies confirmed
the previous appraisal mission's views about the unsuitable geological condi-
tions at the lower dam site. A feasibility report for the selected upper dam
site was completed in October 1978. Field investigations of the irrigation
service area were carried out by ID and FAO culminating in a "Draft Report of
the Nyaunggyat Dam Irrigation Project," prepared by FAO in 1976 and revised
in 1978. These thorough investigations, studies, and reports formed the basis
of the subsequent project appraisal by IDA, along with representatives of
three potential co-financiers (KfW, ODA, and Norway) in April-May 1979. This
appraisal then led to the proposed project that is based on sound engineering
standards and has dimensions of all major structures optimized and defined.
Dam Site and Location
4.03      Extensive field explorations have been accomplished on the proposed
Nyaunggyat project during the past ten years. The work has consisted of local
and regional surface mapping and subsurface core drilling, test pit excavation,
and tunnel driving. The result of this effort was the selection of the most
appropriate site for the main dam and the water holding capability of the
reservoir basin. The dam would be located in a narrow gorge formed by the
Panlaung River as it cuts sharply through a ridge of jurassic quartzite
capped by limestone of the same geologic age (Map IBRD 14544).
Optimization of Project Features
4.04      Once the dam site was decided, the project features were optimized
for the maximum operating level of the reservoir; the subsequent selection



- 18 -
of the dam crest, elevation and spillway capacity, location and dimension of
the other structures, and the determination of the power plant's installed
capacity were all based on economic comparisons. The maximum operating level
of the reservoir was fixed at 680 ft (dam crest 700 ft), which was the switch-
ing value level (incremental benefits equal to incremental costs) with irri-
gation alone as well as with both irrigation and power.
Geology
4.05      Main Dam.  A sound metamorphosed sedimentary rock foundation exists
at the main dam site that would support the proposed structure to the maximum
height desired. Minor faulting and joints are closely spaced, complex, and
erratically orientated; but groundwater levels and water pressure percolation
tests in the foundation drill holes indicate the jointing is well-cemented and
relatively impermeable. The quartzite rock segments, although fractured, are
angular and thus very stable under conditions of loading.
4.06      Allied Structures.  The three dike structures, S1, S2$ and S3 (Map
IBRD 14544), would be founded on a shallow depth of overburden on intrusive
igneous rock and geologically, the foundation conditions are generally quite
favorable. Other structures, including the powerhouse, service and emergency
spillways, and the diversion, irrigation, and power tunnel, would be founded
predominantly on hard and jointed quartzite rock, qualifying as sound found-
ation material. Grouting to moderate depths would be necessary beneath the
main dam and allied structures.
4.07      Reservoir Area.  Surface geological studies of the proposed reservoir
basin indicate its water holding capability would be satisfactory. There are
outcrops of karstic limestone formations in the basin area, but the existing
faulting and lenticular bedding, their elevations, inclinations, and ground-
water characteristics lead to the conclusion that there would be no continuous
seepage paths and that no leakage from the reservoir would be expected through
these limestone formations.
4.08      Re-regulating Dam.  Geological exploration at the re-regulating
dam site just upstream from Kinda Weir has revealed unfavorable foundation
conditions. Proposals are now under study for constructing a re-regulating
dam at Kinda Weir for re-regulation pondage. The detailed proposals are
expected to be finalized with assistance from LI by May 1980.
Seismicity
4.09      The proposed Nyaunggyat project area is located within an active
seismic area (Map IBRD 14543). Three disastrous earthquakes, having inten-
sities of 8-9 on the Modified Mercalli (MM) scale, were recorded during the
last 150 years with epicenters within a radius of 100 mi of the project site.
Project structures would be designed for safe seismic loading with horizontal
and vertical accelerations of 0.25 and 0.083 g, respectively, acting contin-
uously and simultaneously. These accelerations correspond to an earthquake
registering 8-9 on the MM scale at the dam site, which is considered quite
adequate and safe for design of project structures.



- 19 -
Hydrology
4.10      The Panlaung River drains about 49,440 M cu ft (1,400 M m3 ) of
runoff yearly from about 865 sq mi of the catchment area upstream of the dam
site, which has an average annual rainfall of about 42 in. The river basin,
located in the rain-shadow area, has a karstic limestone base with consid-
erable underground storage capable of trapping rainwater and subsequently
releasing trapped water into baseflow. Only a few major floods have occurred
during this century; the maximum peak was recorded in 1926 estimated at 60,000
cusecs. Historical flood data may not be adequately reliable due to poor
gauging procedures. Based on a more reliable regional statistical approach,
estimated flood peaks would range from 60,000 cusecs for a 25-year flood,
67,000 cusecs for 50-year, 76,000 cusecs for 100-year, 106,000 cusec fo 
1,000-year, 141,000 cusecs for 10,000-year, and 229,500 cusecs (6,500 m /sec)
as the Probable Maximum Flood (PMF).
Sedimentation
4.11      Estimates of sediment loads transported by the river into the pro-
posed Nyaunggyat reservoir were based on samplings of the suspended loads
and on the proportion of the bed-loads. The total load, which is equal to
an average annual depth of erosion of about 0.012 in. over the catchment
area above the dam site, agrees with that observed in other basins in the
region with similar physical characteristics. Mean annual sediment inflow
into the Nyaunggyat reservoir would be about 830,000 tons and the volume of
sediment trapped in 100 years will be about 86 M cu yd. The dead storage of
the reservoir would be set at El. 551 ft (168 m) to accommodate this volume,
which provides more than ample sediment storage.
Construction Materials
4.12      General.  Between 1971-76 many field and laboratory investigations
were performed to confirm the suitability and availability of sufficient
earth and rock construction materials for both the main dam and dikes. This
program was continued during 1977-79 while LI prepared the feasibility study,
with additional exploration including drill holes along the dam alignment and
affiliated structures, supplemented by several pits of shallow depth, and
about 20 deep trenches excavated by equipment (depths varying from 8.2-16.4
ft) for core and shell materials.
4.13      Core/Shell Materials.  Impervious core material is found along the
Panlaung River as "Terra Rosa" deposits and as colluvium along the lower por-
tions of the side slopes in the reservoir basin just upstream from the axis
on both sides of the river channel. This material is adequately located in a
stretch of at least 1.4 mi on the left river bank and approximately 0.6 mi on
the right bank. The range of grain size distributions of this CL-type soil
is adequate and quite favorable. The slope wash material of quartzite and
intrusive origin that forms the lower slopes of both the river banks, consists
of a sandy matrix of considerably higher shear strength and permeability and
is suitable outer shell material.



- 20 -
4.14      Rockfill/Riprap.  In the hills on the right river bank, large ex-
posures of in situ quartzite rock confirm that a sufficient amount of free
draining material would be available for rockfill within a short distance of
the dam site. Quartzite, limestone, and diorite rock outcrops are found near
all the project features, and each one would provide a quarry source for
riprap, rockfill, and an alternate source for concrete aggregate. The lime-
stone cap above the present camp site is proposed as the source of riprap
material.
4.15      Concrete Aggregates and Filter Material.  Sand and gravel filter
material and concrete aggregates would be available from deposits near Kanzi
located 3-4 mi downstream from the dam. The strength of concrete cubes cast
and tested has been found to be fairly high. A program of further tests on
aggregates and filter materials is in progress. Crushing of cobbles; washing,
fractioning, and crushing of oversized particles; and selection of particular
fraction sizes will be necessary to obtain optimum concrete mixes.
4.16      Additional Explorations.  To pinpoint and classify additional
sources of natural gravel and rock and other construction materials, addi-
tional exploration and extensive field work would be required to outline and
designate the borrow areas specifically. These explorations pertaining to
(a) construction materials, (b) powerhouse and spillway foundations, and
(c) concrete mix, needed for final design and construction specifications,
are expected to be completed by July 1980.
Reservoir Operation
4.17      Reservoir operation studies were performed for the selected project
variant with a maximum operating level of 680 ft (207.25 m). The monthly
reservoir operation studies were computerized and were based on historical
inflows/outflows (1950-77), corrected for lateral inflow between the dam
site and Kinda Weir, evaporation, seepage losses, and future sedimentation.
Reservoir releases would primarily correspond to irrigation demand. During.
periods when no water would be required for irrigation, water would be released
to maintain power output. The minimum daily operation of the Nyaunggyat power
plant would be based on six hours of generation per day, while the turbine
discharge must be supplied to the irrigation system at a constant rate. The
final reservoir operation studies demonstrate that guaranteed peak power
production would equal about 28 MW at a 90% probability level or 25 MW at a
98% probability level. The average monthly peak generating capacity would be
about 44 MW, and the average annual energy would be about 165 GWh.
V. THE PROJECT
General
5.01      The proposed project would be the first phase of a long-term pro-
gram to develop the land and water resources of the Panlaung and Samon River
valleys in Kyaukse, Myittha, Tada-U, and Wundwin Townships located in Burma's
central dry zone. The project would provide year-round irrigation capability
for approximately 200,000 ac of land. A major part of the proposed area



- 21 -
(88,000 ac), located mainly on the right bank of the Panlaung River and
in the area between the two rivers, is under an existing system commanded by
Kinda Weir, which currently can only supply irrigation during the monsoon
season for one crop. New lands to be developed by the project are generally
further upstream along the left bank of the Samon River.
Project Objectives
5.02      The primary objectives of the project would be to provide a comple-
mentary agricultural development program geared to increase production of paddy,
cotton, oilseeds-, and other crops by increasing yields and cropping intensi-
ties. The project would provide for the rehabilitation of the existing ineffi-
cient and severely silted-up irrigation system, construction of irrigation
facilities for new areas, flood control and drainage improvements, on-farm
development works, improved water management, and research and extension. The
project would alsd provide for hydro-electric power generation with a maximum
generating capacity of 56 MW and an average annual energy production of about
165 GWh.
Project Components
5.03      The project, to be implemented over a six-year period by GOB agen-
cies listed below, would include the following components:
(a) Irrigation Department (ID)
(i)  Construction of a zoned earthfill/rockfill dam with
an impervious core about 236 ft (72 m) in height,
and allied structures.
(ii) Rehabilitation of the existing irrigation and drainage
network (currently serving 88,000 ac on a seasonal
basis).
(iii) Construction of new irrigation facilities for the dev-
elopment of about 113,500 ac.
(iv) Construction of about 110 mi of canal maintenance
roads for use also as farm roads.
(v)  Improvement of existing khals and excavation of new
drainage channels over a total length of about 100 mi.
(vi) Construction of about 32 mi of earth embankments
along the Panlaung and Paukchaung Rivers for flood
protection.
(vii) Provision of construction, O&M, and workshop equipment;
vehicles; buildings; and O&M services.
(viii) Resettlement of persons displaced from the reservoir area,
including compensation for loss of immovable private and
communal property (para 7.29).



- 22 -
(ix) installation of exploratory wells including provision of
tubewell equipment for Phase II of the UNDP/IDA Umbrella
Project pilot groundwater scheme.
(b) Electric Power Corporation (EPC)
(i) Construction of a powerhouse including intake structure
and headrace tunnel, and procurement and installation
of two generating units of 28 MW each and other ancillary
equipment.
(ii)  Construction of about 31 mi (50 km) of 132 kV trans-
mission line to connect Nyaunggyat plant to Grid
system and extension of the Thazi substation.
(iii) Construction of re-regulating dam.
(iv) Preparation of detailed engineering and designs for
the Paunglaung hydroelectric project.
(c) Agriculture Corporation (AC)
Ci) Strengthening of extension services by introduction
of Training and Visit (T&V) programs; construction
of offices, quarters, training center, godowns, and
workshops; establishment of a cotton research farm
and demonstration plots; and provision of transport
and equipment for extension activities.
(d) Agricultural Mechanization Department (AMD)
(i) Provision of equipment for AMD tractor workshops
and for land clearing and rough land levelling.
5.04      The project would also provide for technical assistance and training
(para 5.26).
Detailed Features
A. Nyaunggyat Dam and Reservoir
5.05      Main Dam and Dikes.  The main dam (an earth and rockfill structure)
with a central core and appurtenant spillway and outlet works would be con-
structed on the Panlaung River about 9 mi upstream from the existing Kinda
Weir.  Three saddle dikes, S, S2, and S3 (Map IBRD 14544), varying in height
from about 70 to 125 ft, wouId be required to contain the reservoir.  The
construction of the main dam and saddle dikes at the proposed sites would
provide a reservoir with a total storage of about 34,300 M cu ft at the maxi-
mum operating level of 680 ft and an active storage of 27,000 M cu ft. The
surface area of this reservoir would be 14 sq mi at the maximum operating
level and six sq mi at the minimum operating level.



- 23 -
5.06      Diversion Works, Headrace Tunnel, and Irrigation Outlet.  The diver-
sion works would consist of a diversion tunnel and upstream and downstream
diversion channels with diversion dikes in the riverbed. After completion of
the project, the diversion tunnel, 1,480 ft (450 m) long and 24.6 ft (7.5 m)
in diameter, would be used as a headrace tunnel for irrigation and power
releases. The headrace tunnel would be structurally divided into three
sections: a concrete-lined section, a steel-lined section, and a manifold
system with bifurcation. An irrigation outlet would be provided through a
control valve in the headrace tunnel (Map IBRD 14544).
5.07      Service Spillway.  The service spillway (Map IBRD 14544) would con-
sist of a gated weir section, an open chute with a flip bucket, and a plunge
pool. The spillway would be designed to discharge safely the estimated 1,000-
year flood, while the combined capacity with the emergency spillway would pass
the PMF. Hydraulic model tests were performed at the Karlsruhe Hydraulic
Laboratory, FRG, to determine accurately the proper shape and size of all
parts of the spillway.
B.   Powerhouse and Affiliated Works
5.08      Powerhouse.  The powerhouse would be a shaft-like, partially under-
ground, concrete structure in sound rock, designed to resist heavy earthquakes
and tailwater pressures during floods. The powerhouse would accommodate two
Francis turbine generating units of 28 MW each, along with auxiliary equipment,
control room, workshops, and other service facilities, utilizing a head of
about 115-220 ft. An overhead travelling 120-ton crane would facilitate the
installation, unloading, and handling of equipment within the powerhouse area.
5.09      A power intake structure would be constructed in an open cut excava-
tion on the left bank immediately upstream of the dam. A single intake tunnel
about 100 ft (30 m) long would connect the intake structure to the diversion
tunnel and then to the inlet manifold of the powerhouse. A re-regulating dam
would be constructed at the existing Kinda Weir site providing a re-regulating
volume of about 60 M cu ft. This would allow the power plant to vary water
flow and thereby meet the grid's peak needs without adversely affecting water
flow for irrigation.
5.10      Transformer, Switchyard, and Transmission Line.  The transformer
bay would consist of a concrete structure connected with bus bar ducts to the
powerhouse. Seven single-phase transformers, including one spare for emergency
purposes, with rated output of 35/3 MVA each would be installed to step up
generating voltage of 11 kV to transmission voltage of 132 kV. The trans-
formers would be connected to the 132 kV switchyard by open-air conductors,
which would consist of one transmission line feeder. The power plant would
be connected to the nearest substation at Thazi (about 31 mi from the power-
house) and thus be integrated into the national hydro-grid system by a single-
circuit 132 kV transmission line (Map IBRD 14546).
C.   Access Road
5.11      An access to the dam site would be provided through construction of
a new 21 mi road that would run from a point near Kume along the Mandalay-



- 24 -
Rangoon highway to the dam site through Kinda Weir. The road alignment has
already been surveyed. The roadway would have a width of 34.5 ft including
shoulders and 14.5 ft pavement, and several bridges and culverts would have
to be built for passage of heavy equipment. To avoid delays in project imple-
mentation and resulting increased project costs, the access road to the dam
site would be completed by force account (para 6.12).
D.   Rehabilitation of Existing Irrigation and Drainage System
5.12      The proposed works for the rehabilitation of the currently irrigated
area A (88,000 ac) include the desilting of canals (640,000 cu yd); excavation
of new distributaries (40,000 cu yd); installation of measuring devices (140);
excavation of drains (700,000 cu yd); construction of flood embankments (2.0 M
cu yd); and repair and replacement of canal structures, road improvement, and
on-farm development. The existing natural drains would be improved in about
63,000 ac of this area by deepening and upgrading drains and excavating
supplementary secondary drains (Annex 2(c), Table 4).
E.   Irrigation Works for New Area Development
5.13      New irrigation systems would extend to areas C, D, E, and F cover-
ing a total area of 113,500 ac (Map IBRD 14545) as follows:
Acres
Area C - Between Area E on the left bank of
the Samon River and the right bank
of the Thin Bon River                           25,000
Area D - Adjacent Area C extending up to
natural drainage                                34,500
Area E - The most upstream area on right and
left banks of Samon River                      25,000
Area F - Adjacent Area D extending up to the
left bank of the Irrawaddy River               29,000
113,500
5.14      Works proposed for the new development area include construction of
the main diversion canal and an irrigation canal system, including canal struc-
tures, excavation of secondary and tertiary drains, road construction along the
main canal, land development works, and on-farm development. The total length
of the main canal would be 78 mi, including about 25 mi of lined channel to
prevent canal seepage losses. Six inverted syphons and one aquaduct would be
provided as major drainage crossings. The total length of distributaries
would be approximately 150 mi, and of minors, 300 mi. Roads along the main
canals measuring about 110 mi would be provided for both maintenance of irri-
gation works and usage as farm roads (Annex 2(c), Tables 4-8).
F.   On-Farm Development
5.15      Proposed on-farm development would mainly be limited to rehabilita-
tion of watercourses, replacement of deficient outlets, and construction of
new watercourses and outlets for improved water control. Within watercourse



- 25 -
areas, water distribution would be improved by extending watercourses to reach
each farm. Additionally, measuring devices would be installed at heads of
main canals and other selected reaches for accurate measurement of discharges
(Annex 2(c), Tables 4-8).
G.   Flood Control Works
5.16      The flood protection works against Panlaung River flooding would be
constructed between Nathwle and Htongyi along the right bank of the Panlaung
River. The Pauk Chaung channel would also be contained within flood embank-
ments. These works would be designed to enable the Panlaung River downstream
of the dam site to carry the 50-year outflow. The designed flood protection
embankments, located at least 100 ft from the river bank, would have a crest
width of 10 ft and upstream/downstream slopes of 1.5 to 1 and would be con-
structed with well-compacted, impervious material. The railway crossing in
the Pauk Chaung would be protected by concrete blocks, and the existing
inadequate escape would be replaced by a new structure (Annex 2(c), Table 4).
H.   Construction and O&M Support
5.17      Because of the general short supply and poor operating condition
of equipment in the country, new equipment in quantities needed to construct
and operate civil works would be provided by the project. The equipment would
be delivered to ID for use in the construction of civil works, as well as for
construction of the access road and other mobilization items for the Nyaunggyat
dam. Procurement of new equipment would include the concurrent purchase of
spare parts to ensure satisfactory operational life. After completion of
construction, heavy construction equipment would be used for O&M.
5.18      Office buildings, residential quarters, garages, godowns, workshops,
and other buildings would be required for O&M of the project. To the extent
practical, these O&M buildings would be constructed at the beginning of the
construction season for use by GOB staff assigned during construction. These
buildings would then be converted for permanent O&M use after construction
activities are completed (Annex 2(c), Table 9).
I.   Agricultural Supporting Services
5.19      For intensive agricultural development, including increased produc-
tion of HYV paddy and large-scale introduction of medium-staple cotton, the
project would strengthen agricultural extension, operate cotton demonstration
plots, establish a central research farm for irrigated cotton, and improve
input supply and agricultural mechanization services (Annex 2(d), Table 1).
5.20      Agricultural Extension.  The Training and Visit (T&V) program would
be introduced in the entire Kyaukse, Myittha, Wundwin, and Tada-U Townships.
The program would require provision of additional staff, use of "contact"
farmers, adoption of regularly scheduled farm visits, periodic training, and
intensification of field activities, including regular field scouting to deter-
mine the numbers and kinds of pests on farmers' crops, particularly cotton.
Initially, Subject Matter Specialists (SMSs) would be trained at Mandalay and



- 26 -
Hlaingdet and in special cases at ARI at Yezin. Village Extension Managers
(VEMs) would then be trained by SMSs focusing on topics relevant to farmers'
field activities during each particular crop season. The project would pro-
vide necessary incremental staff (Annex 2(d) Table 4), training centers,
equipment, and transport to support these efforts (Annex 2(d), Table 2). The
project would also cover incremental pay and allowances of extension staff as
well as associated fuel and maintenance costs.
5.21      Demonstration Plots.  The project would introduce furrow irrigation
with drainage onto ya land within the project area. Since this type of irri-
gation is not currently practiced in Burma, it would be necessary to (a) in-
vestigate water management and agronomic problems; (b) test various cropping
and water management systems; (c) train agricultural staff in furrow irrigated
cropping (particularly of late monsoon medium-staple cotton); and (d) give
demonstrations to farmers. Accordingly, the project would operate about four
demonstration plots in selected areas, to introduce year-round irrigation
either by a gravity system or by pumping from surface or groundwater sources,
thus, providing an excellent base on which to begin larger scale irrigation
upon completion of project works.
5.22      Research.  The existing Lungyaw cotton seed farm is neither easily
accessible nor typical of the newly irrigated ya land on which development of
medium-staple cotton and other cash crops under furrow irrigation would be
concentrated. The project would therefore establish a new irrigation research,
demonstration, and training center on about 100 ac in the new area, which
should be operational by the end of the six-year construction period. These
project area-specific cotton demonstration and research efforts are expected
to complement broader interventions proposed under the UNDP-assisted Cotton
Development Project (US$2.2 M), emphasizing dryland cotton research and devel-
opment at Meiktila, post-harvest technology, adaptability trials, training,
model plots, and pilot promotion campaigns.
5.23      Agricultural Credit.  The proposed Nyaunggyat project contains no
credit component. IDA is currently exploring with GOB the possibility of
formulating a nationwide agricultural credit project. It is anticipated that
this proposed credit project would be implemented by the time of completion
of Nyaunggyat project works and therefore would directly complement agricul-
tural development under the Nyaunggyat project. To permit realization of
agricultural production potential resulting from project investments, assur-
ances were obtained from GOB that it would make arrangements to provide
adequate seasonal and investment credit to project area farmers.
J.  Farm Power Support
5.24      To strengthen the existing agricultural mechanization services
and provide facilities for land clearing and rough land levelling for new
land development in areas C, D, E and F, the project would provide for:
(i) construction of tractor sheds and staff quarters and provision of
workshop equipment and tools for workshops at Myittha, Kyauskse and Wundwin
for repair and servicing of farm tractors; and (ii) procurement of ten
crawler tractors with implements and spares and other equipment for land
clearing and rough land levelling.



- 27 -
K.   Pilot Groundwater Scheme
5.25      The groundwater potential of Burma's dry zone is currently being
investigated under the UNDP/IDA Burma Umbrella Project. As part of these
investigations, about 85 fully developed wells are to be installed having
capacities ranging from 0.5 to 2.0 cusecs. Under Phase I of the pilot devel-
opment scheme, begun in December 1978, drilling and hydrologic equipment, well
materials, and pumps were provided to enable drilling and equipping of 36
