productive wells. The proposed Nyaunggyat project would provide financing
for Phase II, which would include drilling and installation of remaining
wells, provision of tubewell equipment, and related technical assistance.
The second phase would be implemented over a two-year period by ID.

services:

to assist the various project agencies in the above activities, including con-
sultants hired for detailed engineering and design of the Paunglaung hydroelec-
tric project, on terms and conditions satisfactory to IDA.

2/   Consulting firms.



- 29 -
Water Supply and Demand
5.28      Water Supply.  The project would derive its water supply entirely
from the Panlaung River on which the Nyaunggyat dam and reservoir would be
constructed. The runoff from the catchment area upstream from the dam site is
quite variable and available mainly in the monsoon season. Over the 1950-77
period, annual runoff has varied from a minimum of about 34,080 M cu ft (1972)
to a maximum 84,800 M cu ft (1956), compared to an annual average of about
50,000 M cu ft. Runoff would be stored and regulated by the proposed Nyaunggyat
reservoir for year-round irrigation and power generation. Releases from the
proposed Nyaunggyat reservoir of about 48,000 M cu ft yearly would be picked
up at the existing Kinda Weir to serve the irrigated area of the project by
a gravity system. Between the dam and Kinda Weir, additional runoff (about
3,500 M cu ft yearly) is available from the local catchment area of about 77
sq mi. This additional runoff would be regulated by the proposed power re-
regulating dam at Kinda Weir, thus bringing the average total project water
supply for irrigation to about 51,500 M cu ft annually. The above run-off
data, which are critical to the project planning and design, have been
thoroughly reviewed and found to be adequately reliable.
5.29      Demand.  Using 1950-77 month-wise data, irrigation demand was esti-
mated with computerization based on the following assumptions: (a) overall
efficiency of about 40% 1/ for paddy and 45-50% 1/ for other crops (45% for
areas C, D, E, and F and 50% for area A), (b) effective rainfall of about 70-
75% of actual monthly rainfall for paddy and 60-65% for other crops, depending
on actual rainfall intensity. Based on the modified Pennman method, the
estimated annual total demand for irrigation would average about 47,000 M cu
ft (1,340 M m ), compared with the available average total project water sup-
ply of about 51,500 M cu ft. For extremely draught years (e.g., 1960), the
estimated annual total demand for irrigation would average about 53,800 M cu
ft (Annex 1, Tables 5-7). The detailed reservoir operation studies indicate
that the reservoir storage would be sufficient to meet irrigation and power
requirements nearly every year, except in the extremely draught years when
deficit of water would occur during April-May for paddy and upland crops.
This shortage frequency level is acceptable.
5.30      Water Quality.  The overall quality of the water supply to be used
for irrigation is good. The water of the Panlaung River is excellent with
very low salt content (200 ppm) and has been used in the existing service
area over the years without the development of salinity or alkalinity problems.
No problems are anticipated in the new area development. Drains would be con-
structed where needed in the new areas, and drainage improvements would be
made in the currently irrigated area to provide desired control of groundwater
levels.
5.31      Water Rights.  In Burma, all water and land belong to GOB; therefore,
there is no likelihood of water extraction upstream. The return flow from the
1/   Based on field efficiency of 85% for paddy and 65% for other crops,
conveyance efficiency of 70% for areas C, D, E, F, and 75% for A,
and the percolation losses of 0.1 in (2.5 mm) per day.



- 30 -
service areas would be enough to maintain the minimum downstream flow require-
ments of the river towards the main Irrawaddy River, 96 mi below the main
diversion at Kinda Weir.
Environmental Effects
5.32      In Burma there is no schistosomiasis or other similar diseases that
are common in some tropical regions of the world. The only known water-related
disease in the area is malaria. At one time, malaria was practically eliminated,
but in recent years the incidence of the disease has been increasing. This
has been due to a variety of factors, including the evolution of mosquitoes
resistant to DDT and a general slackening of control measures because of inade-
quate staffing and budgeting. The project works are not expected to aggravate
the existing conditions of this malady. Special anti-malarial measures would
be provided under the project at all construction camp sites within the project
area.
5.33      Existing fisheries in the area are negligible, and the project would
have little adverse effect on them. The reservoir would have the potential to
become an important area for fish production if planned pilot fish hatcheries
along the river are developed. Investigations for possible development of
fisheries in the reservoir are proposed by the Fisheries Department of GOB
under its normal program.
5.34      Reduced flooding and improved drainage would improve environmental
and public health conditions in the project area. An intensified extension
program through education and training would stress proper application of
pesticides. The effects of pesticides, however, would be evaluated by a short-
term consultant to be provided under the project and attached to AC. In addi-
tion, the laboratory provided through the project would provide facilities
for testing water and soil samples to monitor the use and effect of pesticides.
Power System Planning and Operations Review
5.35      Although a full power appraisal has not been carried out, it is
clear that several aspects of EPC's operations require immediate attention.
A program to improve some of EPC's planning and operations function would
include:
(a) Assessment of hydro projects to follow Paunglaung based
on available relevant information;
(b) Review and extension of the current power investment plan
from 10 years to about 15 years. The revised investment
plan would include the necessary generating and trans-
mission facilities appropriately timed to meet the load
growth at the minimum cost;
(c) A system-wide operating strategy including protective
relaying, fault separation, and load dispatching schemes;
and
(d) Measures to reduce losses in distribution systems.



- 31 -
Assurances were obtained to ensure that GOB would prepare and submit to IDA
this power system planning and operations review by April 1981.
5.36      To utilize fully the off-peak energy from the Lawpita station at
present and also from the proposed Nyaunggyat plant in future, it is essential
that the operation of the Kyunchaung plant be integrated into the hydrogrid
system. Assurances were obtained from GOB that required actions to utilize
fully the output of the Nyaunggyat plant would be undertaken prior to the
commissioning of the plant. These actions would include: (a) integration of
the Kyunchaung gas turbine with the hydro-grid system to facilitate efficiency
throughout the system; (b) installation of additional transformers at the
Mandalay and Thazi substations; (c) upgrading of the transmission voltage for
the Thazi-Toungoo line from the currently planned 132 kV to 230 kV; and (d)
installation of additional transformer at the Toungoo substation.
VI. PROJECT COSTS AND FINANCING
Project Cost Estimates
6.01      Total project costs over the six-year implementation period (July
1980 to June 1986) are estimated at US$235 M equivalent (K 1,574.6 M). The
foreign exchange component is estimated at US$158.0 M equivalent (K 1,058.6 M),
or about 67% of the total project costs. Local costs, estimated at US$77.0 M
equivalent (K 516.0 M), include about US$13 M equivalent (K 87.1 M) for taxes
and duties on imported equipment and materials. 1/ Cost estimates are based
on detailed designs and quantities for ICB civil works and force account works,
with prices adjusted to expected March 1980 price levels. Unit prices for
imported equipment, vehicles, and foreign consultants are based on recent con-
tract awards and international prices. Costs of ICB civil works for construc-
tion of the dam, powerhouse, and allied structures are based on contractors'
estimates. Costs of force account civil works for irrigation facilities,
flood control and drainage, land clearing, workshops and other buildings, and
erection of the transmission line are based on recent costs for similar works
in Burma. Physical contingencies are estimated at: (a) 15% for ICB civil
works for the dam, powerhouse, and allied structures; (b) 15% for force account
works of irrigation facilities, flood control, and drainage for Areas A, C, D,
and E, and 20% for Area F; (c) 15% for the access road; and (d) 5% on equip-
ment for all the project components. Price contingencies have been estimated,
based on the latest Bank price escalation projections (January '80), as follows:

for possible country risks, since this will be the first venture involving
International competitive bidding for civil works construction in Burma.

Procurement
6.06      Equipment and Vehicles.  The procurement of mechanical and electrical
equipment to be installed in the powerhouse, switchyard and Thazi substation;
and the supply of transmission line materials (steel towers, conductors, insu-
lators, etc.) would be covered under KfW assistance (para 6.04) and procured
under kfW procurement guidelines. Equipment, vehicles, and spares for force
account works supplied under Japanese assistance would be procured in accordance
with Japan procurement guidelines. Equipment financed by IDA would be pro-
cured by ICB in accordance with IDA guidelines. Equipment, spare parts, and
materials, and groupings thereof, estimated to cost the equivalent of or less
than US$100,000, which are either required urgently or are not suitable for
international tendering, may be procured through prudent shopping (on the
basis of comparing prices from no less than three independent suppliers) by
normal GOB procurement procedures. Such purchases would be limited to a maxi-
mum of US$1.0 M. Because access road completion is critical to timely and
effective execution of the project, essential equipment for road construction



- 34 -
may be procured after comparing prices from not less than three independent
suppliers in accordance with procurement procedures of the Borrower accept-
able to IDA. Such procurement shall not exceed the equivalent of US$1.0 M.
Tractors, power tillers, other equipment, and field vehicles with an estimated
foreign exchange cost of about US$1.6 M (including contingencies) would be
reserved for procurement under local procedures and would not be eligible for
reimbursement out of the proceeds of the IDA Credit.
6.07      Civil Works.  The major civil works contract (about US$70.0 M) for
construction of the dam, powerhouse, and allied structures, including furnish-
ing and installation of spillway gates and irrigation outlet, would be tendered
for ICB in accordance with IDA guidelines. Necessary steps have been taken by
GOB for prequalifying bidders for ICB works. The bidding documents for the ICB
contract have been prepared and reviewed by IDA and the ID/EPC engineers. The
Government is in the process of approving these documents and the bidding
procedures and the contract is expected to be signed by late 1980. Procure-
ment of construction equipment, materials and supplies, vehicles, and spares
related to construction of the dam, powerhouse, and allied structures under
the ICB contract (para 7.15) would be the responsibility of the ICB contractor.
6.08      Civil works (about US$25.8 M) for irrigation facilities, flood
control and drainage, land clearing, workshops, agricultural godowns, and
training dormitories would not be suitable for ICB. Since these works would
comprise a number of small, simple, scattered works involving earthwork, land
clearing, minor structures, and small buildings, they are unlikely to interest
foreign contractors. Also, construction would be subject to seasonal interrup-
tions from weather and would have to be phased to minimize interruptions to
agriculture. No private contracting industry exists in Burma. ID, AC, and AMD
would, therefore, carry out these works by force account (para 7.16) for which
these agencies are generally well-experienced.
6.09      Transmission Line.  For construction of the 132 kV transmission line,
supply of all materials (steel towers, conductors, insulators, etc.) would be
made under KfW assistance (paras 6.04 and 6.06). EPC would erect the trans-
mission line by force account and carry out final testing of the power plant
under the supervision of consultants (para 7.16). EPC has had wide experience
in erecting transmission lines since 1968.
Disbursements
6.10      Disbursements from the proposed IDA Credit would be made for:
(a) 100% of foreign expenditures for construction of the dam,
and allied structures under the ICB contract, including
furnishing and installation of spillway gates and irriga-
tion outlet (other than those ICB works financed by
German assistance);
(b) 100% of foreign expenditures (cif) and 100% of local expendi-
tures (ex-factory cost) for equipment, spare parts, and con-
struction materials (other than those financed by Germany and
Japan);



- 35 -
(c)  80% of local expenditures for other equipment and spare parts
procured locally through prudent shopping for force account
work;
(d)  100% of foreign expenditures for consulting services (other
than those financed by Norway and Germany); and
(e)  100% of total expenditures for overseas training (other
than those financed by Germany).
6.11      Disbursements against all items would be fully documented.  During
implementation, the disbursement percentages and categories would be reviewed
by IDA from time to time and amended as necessary. The Director of the
Nyaunggyat Project (DNP) (para 7.12) would be responsible for preparing dis-
bursement claims and submitting these to IDA.
6.12      To ensure expeditious completion of the access road by force account
(para 5.11), retroactive financing of up to US$1.5 M is recommended to cover
foreign costs for eligible project expenditures on equipment and materials
incurred from January 1, 1980 up to the date of Credit signing.

Accounts and Audits
6.14      All GOB agencies, including ID, AC, AMD, and EPC, maintain accounts
according to procedures and forms established by the Ministry of Planning and
Finance (MPF). The Central Audit and Inspection Office, acting on behalf of
the Council of People's Inspectors, is responsible for auditing GOB agency
accounts and would be acceptable to IDA as an independent auditor for this
project.  Assurances were obtained from GOB that:
(a)  separate accounts for each project component would be main-
tained by the respective implementing agencies including
the EPC, and collated by the Project Implementation Com-
mittee (PIC);



- 36 -
(b) these accounts would be audited annually in accordance with
sound auditing principles consistently applied by the
Director General, Central Accounts Office, acting on behalf
of the Council of the People's Inspectors of GOB; and
(c) PIC would send to IDA copies of the summary of an unaudited
annual project accounts within four months of the end of the
fiscal year, to be followed by certified copies of the audited
account and auditor's report within nine months of the end of
each fiscal year.
VII. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
Implementation Responsibility
7.01      The Ministry of Agriculture and Forests (MAF) would be responsible
for the implementation of the project. The overall organization of this
ministry is presented in Annex 4, Chart 4. MAF is currently implementing the
Irrigation I Project (Cr. 483-BA); Lower Burma Paddyland Development I Project
(Cr. 642-BA), Seeds Development Project (Cr. 745-BA); Paddyland Development II
Project (Cr. 835-BA); Forestry Project (Cr 493-BA); and Livestock Project
(Cr. 597-BA).
7.02      The various components of the proposed Nyaunggyat project would be
implemented by existing GOB agencies and departments normally concerned with
those activities. Altogether there would be four agencies directly respon-
sible for project implementation, three under MAF and one under the Ministry
of Industries II (MI-2) as detailed below:
GOB Agency                                Ministry
(a)  Irrigation Department (ID)                           MAF
(b)  Agriculture Corporation (AC)                         MAF
(c)  Agricultural Mechanization Department (AMD)          MAF
(d)  Electric Power Corporation (EPC)                     MI-2
In addition, the Planning and Statistics Department (PSD) and the Settlement
and Land Records Department (SLRD), both within MAF, and the Ministry of Plan-
ning and Finance would be directly involved with implementing agencies in proj-
ect coordination and management, while the Survey Department (SD), Cooperative
Department (CD), Agriculture and Farm Products Trade Corporation (AFPTC),
and Myanma Agricultural Bank (MAB) would be indirectly involved with project
implementation.
7.03      The Irrigation Department (ID) would be responsible for:  (a) super-
vising construction of the dam, and allied structures under ICB cQntract; (b)
rehabilitation of existing irrigation works; (c) construction of new irrigation



- 37 -
facilities; (d) construction of flood control works, drainage improvements,
and canal roads; (e) on-farm development and water management; (f) construc-
tion of the access road, O&M buildings, and camp sites; and (g) procurement
of construction equipment and materials for force account works. ID would
organize eight units for executing the project including six units for force
account works (three for civil construction; two for mechanical engineering,
and one for planning and design); and two units for ICB civil works (one for
dam construction and one for testing and quality control). Each unit would
be headed by an ID executive engineer (Annex 4, Chart 2). All the units would
be supervised by two ID superintending engineers under the direct control of
the Director of the Nyaunggyat Project (para 7.12).
7.04      ID has been the pioneer agency in the field of water resources dev-
elopment in Burma. In the past, its responsibilities have included planning,
design, construction, and O&M of both single and multipurpose projects. ID's
current program includes several irrigation and drainage projects. Its per-
manent professional staff is well-qualified to carry out the program success-
fully. However, its operations often suffer from: (a) delays in GOB approval
of additional project staffing, and (b) shortage of adequate equipment, vehic-
les, and spare parts. With the necessary equipment and vehicles to be pro-
vided under the project, ID staff should have no problem implementing the
force account project civil works, provided required additional staffing
is approved expeditiously by GOB.
7.05      The Agriculture Corporation (AC) would be responsible for (a)
strengthening extension services; (b) establishing a cotton research and demon-
stration farm in the newly irrigated area; and (c) constructing input godowns,
staff quarters, dormitories, and farm buildings. AC would provide research
support, implement the intensified extension program, and supply seeds, fer-
tilizers, and other inputs to project farmers. AC would appoint a Project
Extension Manager (PEM), with the grade of AC Deputy General Manager, who
would be headquartered at Myittha and would be responsible for implementation
and coordination of the T&V extension program in the entire four-township
administrative area. Currently, however, each township has a Township Exten-
sion Manager (TEM) who has varied functions (extension, supplies and services,
and other miscellaneous jobs). Therefore, agricultural extension personnel
under the TEM would be divided into two groups: one strictly for the T&V
extension program and one for supplies, services, and miscellaneous jobs;
each group would be under a separate Deputy Township Extension Manager (DTEM).
For the T&V program, which includes field scouting of cotton pests, the DTEM
would supervise the work of 4-5 Village Tract Extension Managers (VTEMs) each
in charge of about 6-7 Village Extension Managers (VEMs). Each VEM would be
responsible for about 350 farm families. The PEM would be assisted by four
Subject Matter Specialists (SMSs) for cotton, paddy, plant protection, and
on-farm water management, all to be stationed at Myittha.
7.06      Present levels of supporting services throughout Burma would be in-
adequate for intensive agricultural development, and GOB must begin improving
services and increasing input supplies in areas designated for intensive dev-
elopment, such as the project area. Assurances were obtained from GOB that:



- 38 -
(a) the organizational set-up and work program for the agricul-
tural extension services for the project would be satis-
factory to IDA;
(b) fertilizers would be supplied for all paddy and cotton
varieties, and for all crops in the project area at rates
to be recommended each year by the extension service; and
(c) each AC Township Supplies and Service Office would stock
sufficient sprayers and pesticides in the project area on
the basis of estimates to be made annually by the exten-
sion service.
7.07      The Agricultural Mechanization Department (AND) would be responsible
for (a) land clearing and levelling in the new development areas; (b) con-
structing staff quarters, tractor sheds, and workshops; (c) providing tractor
services to farmers; and (d) maintaining farm machinery.
7.08      The Electric Power Corporation (EPC) would be responsible for (a)
supervising construction of the powerhouse and allied structures and instal-
lation of equipment; and (b) erecting the 132 kV transmission line. The Man-
aging Director of EPC, under the direction of MI-2, is responsible for the
overall management of the Corporation. He is appointed by the Council of State
on recommendation of the Cabinet and the Chairman of the Management Board of
EPC. This board consists of seven members (including the Chairman) who assist
the Managing Director. The detailed organization chart of EPC is shown in
Annex 4, Chart 5. EPC would mobilize four additional units for the project--
to supervise powerhouse construction, equipment installation including exten-
sion of the Thazi substation, and to construct the 132 kV transmission line.
Each unit would be headed by an EPC executive engineer. These units would
be under the direct control of the Superintending Engineer (EPC) for the
powerhouse and transmission lines (Annex 4, Chart 2).
7.09      EPC has had wide experience since 1968 in constructing transmission
lines. EPC completed the 132 kV Thazi-Chauk line (90 mi) in 1969 and the addi-
tion of one circuit (70 mi) to the 132 kV Lawpita-Thazi line (under energized
condition) in 1975 by force account. The 66 kV transmission line, recently
completed from Myanaung to Myaungmya (160 mi), was also erected by force
account. Based on past performance, EPC has the capability of constructing
the project transmission line by force account, provided required additional
project staff is approved by GOB.
Project Management and Coordination
7.10      To coordinate the work of the various implementing agencies, a
Project Implementation Committee (PIC) would be formed in MAF similar to
the PICs for the Lower Burma Paddyland Development Projects I and II. The
proposed Nyaunggyat project however, involves complex components and multiple-
agencies, and successful implementation would depend heavily upon the capabil-
ity for quick resolution of policy issues and bottlenecks encountered during
implementation. PIC, chaired by the Deputy Minister of MAF would, therefore,
be limited to only representatives of those agencies directly involved in



- 39 -
project implementation, with due representation of EPC. Representatives of
other related agencies would be associate members to be called by the chairman
as needed. The proposed PIC organization is presented in Annex 4, Chart 2.
7.11      PIC would be responsible for resolution of inter-ministerial project-
related policy issues and implementation bottlenecks as well as for other
important administrative matters. PIC would be responsible for: (a) reviewing
and approving annual work programs and budgets; (b) reviewing quarterly
progress reports; and (c) conferring with IDA on all major project-related
policy matters.
7.12      A competent senior ID officer within MAF would be appointed Director
of the Nyaunggyat Project (DNP) and would serve concurrently as member-secretary
of PIC. He would be responsible for coordinating technical planning and field
execution of the project's various components. The DNP would be assisted by
three superintending engineers, two from ID for dam and allied structures
construction under ICB contract and the other for irrigation and agricultural
development works by force account, and one from EPC for the power plant and
transmission lines (Annex 4, Chart 2). DNP's responsibilities would include:
(a) preparation of annual work programs and budgets in col-
laboration with the various implementing agencies;
(b) coordinate the release of funds and payments for project
expenditures by the respective agencies;
(c) coordinate the preparation and maintenance of consolidated
project accounts;
(d) coordinate in the preparation of claims for reimbursement
from the proposed financing package;
(e) monthly progress review with each implementing agency;
(f) resolution of implementation bottlenecks; and
(g) conferring with PIC on all project matters.
7.13      The proposed Nyaunggyat project would require establishment of three
additional circles, each headed by a superintending engineer, and 11 additional
units 8 for ID (para 7.03) and 3 for EPC (para 7.08)). GOB has approved the
three additional circles and has appointed the three (2 ID, and 1 EPC) Superin-
tending Engineers. GOB has also appointed a full-time Director for Nyaunggyat
Project and has established the PIC. Assurances were obainted from GOB that
required additional staff for ID, AC, and EPC for project implementation would
be approved and appointed not later than October 31, 1980 in accordance with
the agreed schedule with IDA.
7.14      Coordination of land allotments and/or redistribution in the project
area would be carried out by the township councils, which are responsible for
civil administration and land allocation. Each township would have a Township



- 40 -
Project Committee (TPC) to coordinate land allotments, extension activities,
and construction. Each TPC would include the township-level officers of the
implementing agencies as members, the ID Executive Engineer as chairman, and
the AC township-level manager as member-secretary. After the completion of
project construction, the AC Township Manager would serve as chairman, with one
other member of the Committee serving as secretary. TPCs would be established
before construction and land reclamation begin in the respective townships.
ICB and Force Account Works
7.15      ICB Works.  The principal feature of this multipurpose project would
be the construction of major structures including the dam, dikes, spillway,
powerhouse, and allied structures. Timely, efficient, and proper construction
of these specialized structures would demand the services of an experienced
contractor (a foreign contractor since there are no contractors in Burma).
It was agreed between GOB and IDA that the construction of the dam, power
plant, and appurtenant structures would be undertaken by ICB contract in
accordance with the IDA's normal procedures.
7.16      Force Account Works.  The following works would be implemented by
ID through force account: (a) rehabilitation of existing irrigation works;
(b) construction of new irrigation facilities; (c) construction of flood
control works and drainage improvements; (d) on-farm development and water
management; and (e) construction of the access road, canal roads, campsites,
and O&M buildings. Staff quarters, input godowns, dormitories, and farm
buildings would be constructed by AC; staff quarters, tractor sheds, and
workshops by AMD; and the 132 kV transmission line by EPC.
7.17      All implementing agencies are generally well-experienced with the
type of work included in the project and have been involved in the past with
similar, successfully completed works. With additional staffing and technical
assistance to be provided under the project (para 5.26), all agencies are
expected to be fully capable of implementing their respective components.
Engineering and Designs
7.18      Feasibility designs and estimates for the dam, powerhouse, and
allied structures were made by LI, who have done extensive work in other
countries of Southeast Asia. Preliminary designs and estimates were made by
LI in their feasibility report of November 1978, which were suitably modified
in April-May 1979 based on additional information on foundation geology and
construction materials. LI is also currently preparing detailed designs
and specifications for ICB bidding documents for construction of the dam,
powerhouse, and allied structures.
7.19      The layout and design of irrigation works to be constructed by force
account have been prepared by ID. ID's civil works designs and estimates for
the project have been based on plan and profile drawings of the main canals
taken from field surveys of the entire project service area, aerial photographs,
and complete surveys of project areas, except Area F. Sample areas were used
to compute work quantities for minor canals, watercourses, and drains for the



- 41 -
proposed newly irrigated areas. Preliminary designs and estimates were com-
pleted for each major structure including syphons, aquaducts, bridges, falls,
escapes, canal linings and flood embankments. ID has had considerable experi-
ence in planning, designing, and constructing these kinds of engineering
works.
Implementation Schedule
7.20      Overall project implementation would take six years (Annex 4, Chart 1).
However, the dam, powerhouse, and allied structures are expected to be com-
pleted by the end of the fifth year. Site surveys for most buildings for the
project are already underway, and construction of these buildings should be
completed in about two years. Tender documents for construction and other
equipment for works by force account have been prepared, and the contracts
are expected to be awarded by July 1980 with site deliveries in late-1980.
Staff mobilization is expected to begin by mid-1980.
7.21      The preliminary works related to construction of the dam, powerhouse,
and allied structures under the ICB civil works contract and much of the sup-
porting infrastructure for agricultural development provided through force
account would be completed within two years of the project's start. Emphasis
in the remaining years would be on rehabilitating irrigation works, construct-
ing irrigation facilities for new areas, introducing irrigation water manage-
ment, intensifying agricultural development, and completing the dam, power-
house, allied structures, and transmission line.
7.22      By the end of September of each year, DNP in collaboration with the
various implementing agencies, would prepare a detailed work program for the
following calendar year, including associated budgetary requirements. This
program would be supported by appropriate maps, charts, and diagrams and would
include estimated manpower and material requirements. Following a review by
PIC, the work program would be submitted to IDA for comments. Assurances were
obtained from GOB that by October 31 of each year, IDA would receive for com-
ment the detailed work program for the following calendar year.
Monitoring and Evaluation
7.23      The project's progress would be reported monthly to DNP by the
staff of the various implementing agencies. To avoid burdening field staff
with excessive paperwork, monthly progress reports should be brief and include
only data of key importance. DNP would then consolidate these individual
reports into quarterly reports for review and discussion by PIC. Formats for
these quarterly reports would be worked out jointly by DNP and the concerned
agencies with assistance from IDA. Quarterly reports, together with other
relevant project records, would be used to prepare a comprehensive annual
progress report. Assurances were obtained from GOB that quarterly progress
reports would be submitted to IDA within one month of the end of the quarter
under review and that annual progress reports would be sent to IDA by March 31
of the following year.



- 42 -
7.24      To aid MAF in the agricultural development of the project area and
to provide a basis for the future evaluation of the project, a monitoring pro-
gram would be undertaken.  Township councils routinely maintain records from
which much of the pertinent information could be compiled. However, agricul-
tural surveys would be required to supplement this information and to provide
a consistent data base for monitoring agricultural development. An assurance
was obtained from GOB that PIC would establish a project monitoring unit to
conduct benchmark surveys every two years beginning January 1981, covering,
inter alia, cropped areas, yields, use of farm inputs, and project-related
changes in area employment opportunities and in farm and family incomes. An
assurance was also obtained from GOB that it would maintain records and proce-
dures adequate to record and monitor the progress of the project (including
costs and benefits), and furnish to IDA at regular intervals all such inform-
ation as IDA shall reasonably request concerning the project, its cost, and
benefits derived.
Operation and Maintenance
7.25      The installation of project works, including the dam, allied struc-
tures, irrigation facilities, flood embankments, and drains, and the addition
of about 113,500 ac of service area would greatly expand O&M activities in the
Panlaung River irrigation system. The system is currently managed by one main-
tenance engineer with four sub-assistants supervising four sub-areas and one
sub-assistant for special duty. For current operations and water distribution,
there are two canal inspectors, four assistants, and supporting staff. Mainte-
nance is accomplished primarily by hand labor supplemented by heavy mainte-
nance equipment stationed at ID base workshops. Estimates of incremental O&M
buildings and staff are provided in Annex 2(c), Table 9. Provision is made
under the project to meet these needs to ensure proper O&M.
7.26      The dam, allied structures, re-regulating dam, irrigation facili-
ties, campsites, and residential quarters for ID construction and maintenance
units would be maintained by ID; fertilizer godowns, residential quarters for
extension staff, and training school buildings by AC; farm equipment workshops
by AMD; and the power plant and transmission lines by EPC. O&M costs during
the six-year implementation period have been included as a GOB contribution
to the project cost.
7.27      ID would also be responsible for reservoir operation in collabora-
tion with EPC based on requirements for irrigation and power generation.
Assurances were obtained from GOB that detailed regulations for reservoir
operation would be submitted to IDA by September 1983 for review and comment.
Assurances were also obtained from GOB that: (a) the dam, allied structure,
powerhouse, transmission line, and all irrigation facilities, roads, and
buildings constructed and rehabilitated under the project would be maintained
in accordance with sound engineering practices by the respective agencies
including ID, AC, AMD, and EPC; (b) adequate staff and funds would be provided
for this purpose; (c) annual budgetary allocations for the various implement-
ing agencies would be appropriately increased to provide for project-related
recurrent expenses; and (d) EPC would perform its operations, plan future
development of its power system, and maintain its financial position in accor-
dance with sound engineering, financial, and administrative practices under the



- 43 -
supervision of experienced and competent management, and that EPC would furnish
to IDA unaudited financial statements for its operation within four months of
the end of each fiscal year to be followed by certified copies of its financial
statements and audit reports within nine months of the end of each fiscal year.
Dam Safety Program
7.28      The International Commission on Large Dams, as well as all the various
dam project agencies throughout the world, are becoming increasingly concerned
about dam safety since dam failures have often involved disastrous consequences.
The Bank Group has also taken a constructive step in this regard by preparing
some useful guidelines. To ensure the safety of the proposed Nyaunggyat dam,
the project would provide for the following specific safety measures:
(a) Instrumentation. The main dam, saddle dikes, and allied
structures would be equipped with instrumentation to fore-
cast serious damage or movements by measuring strains,
settlements, internal seepage pressures, and seepage
gradients. Such instrumentation for the main dam and
dikes would principally consist of: (i) benchmarks for
geodetic control; (ii) vertical and horizontal settle-
ment gauges; (iii) soil pressure and pore water gauges;
(iv) embankment and foundation piezometers; and (v) seismo-
graphs. Three complete measuring sections from upstream
to downstream of the dam embankment, would be installed
with the above instrumentation. The proposed instrumen-
tation would provide handy tools for studying the behavior
of the dam and foundations after construction and for
timely requisite protective measures to control undesir-
able developments or emergency conditions.
(b) Advisory Panel of Experts. The proposed project would provide
for an Advisory Panel of Experts to carry out a comprehensive
review of the designs and/or completed works of the dam and
related structures with the objective of ascertaining and
reporting on their general safety and performance, with
detailed consideration of structural, geologic, hydraulic,
hydrologic, and soil mechanics aspects. The proposed
three-member panel would include: (i) a dam design expert;
(ii) a dam construction engineer; and (iii) an engineering
geologist. The panel would meet often enough for it to
ensure general safety of the dam and allied structures and
suggest safeguarding measures and/or treatments as and when
necessary during various stages of dam construction, con-
struction designs refinement, and safety inspections of the
dam. Assurances were obtained from GOB that the members of
the Advisory Panel of Experts for dam safety would be appointed
by GOB on terms and conditions satisfactory to IDA, prior to the
award of the ICB civil works contract.
Resettlement
7.29      Project works would necessitate almost negligible relocation of
persons from affected areas. The construction of the Nyaunggyat dam would



- 44 -
entail the flooding of four villages (Neyaunggar, Nyaunggyat, Taungbon, and
Patchaung) in the reservoir area having a total population of about 400 and
including about 650 ac of rainfed cultivable land. The project would provide
for the resettlement of these people including compensation for loss of immov-
able private property and communal property (pagodas and monasteries), trans-
port for movable private property, and subsistence allowance for about six
months (Annex 2(a), Table 1). The project would include development and
irrigation of about 11,000 ac of bush land in the dryland tract onto which
this small number of dislocated people could be re-established. At these
sites, affected families would have greater income potential and would be
provided health care and other amenities considerably better than in their
present malarial environment. Assurances were obtained from GOB that a
resettlement program, including adequate compensation, would be carried out
for persons affected by dam construction in accordance with a resettlement
plan acceptable to IDA.
VIII. PRODUCTION, MARKETING, FARM INCOMES, AND COST RECOVERY
Cropping Patterns
8.01      The project would improve agricultural production on 88,000 ac which
are currently irrigated and on 113,500 ac of rainfed area to be brought under
irrigation. Approximately 11,000 ac of this rainfed area is bush land that
would be developed with the project. In terms of land categories (para 2.04),
this would comprise 84,400 ac of paddyland and 110,300 ac of ya land. Through
provision of year-round irrigation, the project would enable development of
two distinct cropping patterns: one for paddyland emphasizing paddy cultiva-
tion with basin irrigation, and one for ya land based on medium-staple cotton
production with furrow irrigation.
8.02      Paddyland Cropping Pattern.  The 84,400 ac of paddyland (68,000 ac
in the rehabilitation area and 16,400 ac in the newly irrigated area) would
have paddy as the dominant crop. It is expected that with the development of
medium-staple cotton on the newly irrigated ya land, it would no longer be
necessary for GOB to require cultivation of pre-monsoon cotton on 30% of irri-
gated paddyland, which lacks proper drainage. This, together with an assured
year-round supply of irrigation water, would enable farmers to increase area
under HYV paddy for both pre-monsoon and monsoon crops. Accordingly, cotton
would disappear from paddyland, and cropped area under paddy would increase
from about 52,000 to 107,000 ac, while the area under residual crops would
remain basically unchanged. With the project, cropping intensity would in-
crease on average from about 123% to 167% as shown below (Annex 1, Table 8):

8.03      Ya Land Cropping Pattern.  Of the 110,300 ac of ya land, 20,000
ac of currently irrigated land would be rehabilitated and 90,300 ac would
be newly irrigated. Since ya land is largely well-drained, introduction of
furrow irrigation would permit development of medium-staple cotton as a late
monsoon crop, complemented by other cash crops such as chillies, oilseeds,
and onions. It is expected that the area under cotton would increase from the
current 10,100 ac (3,200 ac medium-staple and 6,900 ac short-staple) to 73,700
ac (medium-staple) at full development. Area under other cash crops would
also increase from 36,300 ac to 87,100 ac. However, the area under pulses
would decrease from 27,900 to 15,500 ac. The current paddy area of about
4,600 ac would be replaced by the above crops. In general, the cropping
intensity would increase from about 75% to 160% at full development as

Yields and Production
8.04      Apart from permitting more use of HYV paddy seeds and development
of the late monsoon crop of medium-staple cotton, assured year-round irri-
gation would make larger and more effective applications of chemical fertil-
izers and pesticides financially rewarding. Use of fertilizers in the area
is expected to increase from 975 tons at present to an estimated 21,675
tons--an amount consistent with good cultivation practices. Accordingly,
average yields would increase from about 0.69 tons to about 1.51 tons per ac
for paddy and from 0.19 tons to 0.68 tons per ac for cotton. Yields of
chillies, sesame, groundnut, pulses, and onions would also increase substan-
tially. Present and projected yields are presented in Table 8.3.

Marketing and Pricing of Project Output
8.06      The project would contribute to increased surpluses for export
(rice) and save foreign exchange through import substitution (cotton). Pro-
duction increases of other crops (chillies, onions, sesame, groundnuts) would
be essentially small relative to present output nationwide and would have a
ready domestic market (paras 2.28 and 8.21).
8.07      Paddy.  GOB procures paddy from farmers through the Agriculture
and Farm Produce Trade Corporation (AFPTC) based on compulsory minimum quotas.
Since paddy is procured at about 33-50% below open market prices and about
60% below its adjusted international value, the procurement system also serves
as an agricultural taxation mechanism. Progressive in nature, the system and
its administered pricing regime decrease production incentive. Over the 1975-
79 period, AFPTC has procured an average of 2.9 M tons of paddy p.a., or about
one-third of national production. While targets and guidelines are established
in Rangoon, actual procurement quotas are fixed at township and village tract
levels. Each farmer's quota is based on sown acreage and yields as well as
his estimated surplus after allowing for family consumption, seed, labor
in-kind payments, and losses. A production estimate is made by the Settle-
ment and Land Records Department (SLRD) based on two field surveys of the
crop. Surveyors generally keep yield estimates conservative to avoid farmer
complaints (para 8.31). A farmer who does not meet his quota is subject to
sanctions, including refusal of mills to process his paddy and recently, loss
of cultivation rights (para 2.10).



- 48 -
8.08      The farmer is free to sell surplus above the quota within his town-
ship at prevailing free market prices. Sale of paddy or rice across township
boundaries is restricted, however authorities tolerate private sales of small
quantities, which are often of higher quality than rice officially distributed,
into cities and deficit areas at free market prices (para 8.07). Since the
procurement system skims off on average about 60% of marketable surplus, the
average farmer can sell the remaining surplus at considerably higher prices.
Therein lies a real incentive for the farmer to adopt HYV packages and improve
overall productivity, particularly in rice-deficit areas. In fact, nation-
wide, during the 1971-78 period (a) area sown to HYVs more than doubled;
(b) HYVs as a percent of total area sown to paddy increased from 8% to 15%;
and (c) the quantity of chemical fertilizers applied to paddy practically
trebled (para 1.07).
8.09      GOB-Controlled Paddy Pricing and Production Incentives.  The rice
sector dominates the Burmese economy, involving about 70% of all farmers,
accounting for 40% of export earnings, and contributing perhaps 75-80% of
total caloric intake. Indicative of the commodity's importance was the
establishment in 1977 of a Rice Commission, chaired by the Minister of Agri-
culture and Forests, to examine all aspects of rice production, marketing,
and incentives. Its deliberations are unusually sensitive since rice pricing
is one of the most important policy issues in Burma.
8.10      With stagnating paddy production and declining rice exports in the
early and mid-1970s GOB became increasingly aware of the need to remedy trends.
Programs designed to boost productivity are now underway or in preparatory
stages to alleviate current shortages of fertilizers, pesticides, and certified
seeds; to expand research and extension; to encourage cultivation of HYVs; to
reclaim and irrigate more land; and to increase and upgrade storage, process-
ing, and transport capacity. The principal thrust of GOB's strategy has been
on input supply incentives supplemented by organizational and technical sup-
port. To encourage yield-augmenting input use, GOB provides fertilizers and
pesticides to growers at heavily subsidized rates. Estimates of subsidy levels
range from about 15% for muriate of potash, 50% for pesticides, and 66% for
urea. With rising international prices, rigidity in controlled input price
levels would lead, of course, to higher subsidy levels (Annex 3, Table 14 and
paras 8.28 and 8.29). In addition to providing input subsidies, GOB attempts
to trim grower costs further by organizing crews of volunteer workers to assist
farmers in planting and harvesting operations. GOB's inclination toward input
support mechanisms and its reticence toward price incentives reflect a basic
sensitivity to the possible inflationary consequences that modifications to
the official procurement and distribution systems might trigger. Similarly,
implications for the state budget of paddy pricing policy adjustments are
serious concerns in Burma where government resources are scarce and develop-
ment programs require considerable expansion. In addition, even though GOB
increased the official retail rice price by 187% over the 1972-77 period, a
basic tenet of Burma's development strategy has been to keep retail prices
low, not just to meet social objectives, but also to support industrializa-
tion by keeping wage costs down.



- 49 -
8.11      Since its inception, the Rice Commission has made three recommend-
ations to GOB: (a) raise basic paddy procurement prices by 22%; (b) give
even higher rates (2-6%) for premium quality paddy/rice; and (c) adjust the
procurement system to acquire 100% of marketable surplus. To date GOB has
not moved on the first suggestion, but effective October 1977 instituted a
system of higher procurement prices (about 4%) for first grade deliveries
to AFPTC collection centers. Regarding revisions to the paddy procurement
system, GOB is currently experimenting in four townships with 100% procure-
ment of surplus with existing procurement prices. To induce deliveries beyond
traditional quotas, GOB is reportedly supplying consumer goods at subsidized
rates.
8.12      With the exception of the above-mentioned marginal adjustment for
quality paddy/rice, representing only about 15% of AFPTC's procurement intake,
GOB maintains a controlled procurement price regime unchanged since July 1974.
Government programs to increase yield-augmenting input supplies and services
are indeed useful, but paddy price revisions are particularly needed in view
of real price deterioration (1974-78) by about 45-50%. Consideration should
also be given to eventual reduction in input subsidies to limit budgetary
drain. Yet, the importance of the trade-offs between necessary higher pro-
duction and exports on the one hand, and nutrition, inflation, and domestic
resource mobilization on the other should not be minimized. To limit disrup-
tive effects, GOB must introduce future price adjustments gradually and must
strike a judicious balance between multiple, and often conflicting, socio-
economic objectives.
8.13      Rice Milling.  Most of the rice milling capacity in Burma (97%) is
in the private sector. About 25% of the private sector mills (60% of capacity)
are required by law to mill for AFPTC. The remaining installations are permit-
ted to mill farmers' paddy. GOB-regulated mills charge about K 17 per ton of
AFPTC paddy and K 49 per ton of privately-owned paddy. The former charge is
considered by millers inadequate for services rendered--failing to compensate
for the full costs of depreciation, proper O&M, and a reasonable return on
investment. Hence, most mills are old and are operating beyond their economic
life. At the village level, paddy is generally milled at home or at small
artisanal facilities. Until recently, mills have suffered from spare parts
shortages. However, in 1979 mill owners began to receive needed spare parts
at a 50% subsidy through AFPTC. Nevertheless, the milling sector is charac-
terized by generally poor maintenance, low milling output (60%), outdated tech-
nology, and disinvestment. AFPTC is currently negotiating with the People's
Republic of China, Japan, and the Asian Development Bank for assistance to the
rice milling sector. Milling capacity in and around the project area, only
about 33% utilized at present, is considered adequate to process incremental
paddy output from the project if spares are made available for maintenance
of older facilities. In addition, the region should benefit substantially
from AFPTC's effort to upgrade national capacity. To complement this nation-
wide program, the project would finance short-term consultants to investigate
project area needs and make recommendations to GOB (para 5.26).
8.14      Rice Exports.  Rice exports dwindled from 3 M tons in the late 1930s
to 183,000 tons in 1975 due to stagnant production, procurement problems, and
increasing domestic requirements. Since then, rice exports have improved,



- 50 -
reaching 646,000 tons in 1978. With a 24% drop in paddy procurement in 1978
due to both farmer dissatisfaction with more forceful procedures adopted to
acquire quotas and high prices on the extra-legal international market, rice
exports for 1979 declined to only about 305,000 tons. However, with 57% higher
procurement levels in 1979 (3.5 M tons), 1980 exports are expected to recover
and to reach about 900,000 tons by 1983. Improvement in export quality through
better processing, storage, and handling facilities should improve prospects
for exports. However, such infrastructure should be complemented with more
aggressive marketing campaigns and longer-term contracting.
8.15      Internal Rice Movements.  The major rice movements are from rice sur-
plus areas in Lower Burma to (a) rice deficit areas in Upper Burma for local
consumption and (b) the ports of Rangoon and Bassein for export. Official
movement of rice totals about 1.6 M tons of which 1.0 M tons represent internal
consumption supplies. The dry zone of Burma, in which the project is situated,
currently produces only about 15% of the national rice crop (0.9 M tons) but
consumes twice as much. Incremental rice produced under the project would
total about 76,200 tons. About 60% of this quantity, equivalent to incremental
GOB procurement, would substitute for about 49,800 tons shipped from GOB
stocks in Lower Burma. An equivalent amount would be available for export.
8.16      Cotton.  AC provides seed and other inputs to cotton growers on
cash payments; the Textile Corporation (TexCorp) under MI-1 makes purchase
advances to farmers who contract to sell cotton and arranges for cotton gin-
ning, by-product processing, marketing of baled cotton, and textile manufac-
ture. At the present time, no seasonal credit is given through MAB for cotton
cultivation. The purchase advance from TexCorp is at the rate of K 210 per
ac with a maximum of K 700 per farmer. At the time of flowering, TexCorp
surveys the farmer's field and advances this partial payment.
8.17      Compulsory Cotton Purchasing and Pricing.  TexCorp buys seed cotton
at designated buying centers at K 7 per viss (K 4,238 per ton) for medium-
staple cotton and an average of K 5 per viss (K 3,027 per ton) for short-staple
cotton. These prices represent the result of substantial price adjustments
made in 1975 and 1977 and are believed by administrators and farmers alike
to offer considerable incentive to increase productivity (para 8.24). In 1978
TexCorp purchased 19,700 tons of seed cotton, about 47% of national production.
Medium-staple cotton comprised about 66% of TexCorp's 1978 purchases, about
52% of national medium-staple production. By 1979, TexCorp had increased
procurement to 30,100 tons, or 57% of total output; medium-staple purchases
represented 57% of all procurement and 68% of total medium-staple cotton
production. Farmers are required to sell all medium-staple cotton to TexCorp
and generally dispose of about 40% of their short-staple cotton at TexCorp
buying depots. Quantities not sold to TexCorp are utilized in local artisanal
industries. Free market prices for cotton are reportedly about twice GOB-
administered levels. TexCorp gins purchased seed cotton in its own facili-
ties, bales the lint cotton, and then sends the lint to its textile mills.
Cottonseed is either allocated for AC seed sales to farmers or is crushed in
TexCorp presses; cottonseed cake is generally sent to AFPTC for export, and
oil distributed internally for industrial purposes (import substitution).



- 51 -
8.18      Cotton Processing.  Ginning facilities, while GOB-owned and operated,
currently suffer from the same kinds of problems observable in the rice mill-
ing sector--poor maintenance, antiquated technology, unserviceable capacity,
and substandard output. Nationwide, TexCorp operates 46 ginning mills, most
of which are equipped with vintage belt-driven roller gins. Equipment is to
a great extent unused for lack of spares. MI-1 proposes an ambitious rehab-
ilitation and modernization program to more than double processed output
nationwide over the 1980-83 period. GOB is currently exploring the possibi-
lity of ADB-financing for a modern ginnery capable of turning out 3,000 lbs
of lint cotton per hour, tentatively proposed for the Myittha area. The pro-
posed ginnery complex would be operating by end-1983 and would produce lint of
international standard. Projected ginning capacity in and around the project
area would be adequate to process incremental project output if the proposed
new facility is supplemented by older units properly maintained through
adequate spares. To complement MI-l's renovation program, the project would
finance short-term consultants to investigate project area needs and make
recommendations to GOB (para 5.24).
8.19      Most of the lint cotton produced in the project area is currently
shipped to the Paleik Textile Mill near Mandalay. Operational since 1972,
this modern facility does not have sufficient raw materials to run even one
full shift. In view of the low utilization of facilities, TexCorp has con-
verted its weaving section to produce a blend of 65% polyester and 35% cotton.
8.20      Import Substitution.  TexCorp estimates the 1978 national demand
for cloth at 354 M yds and supply at 181 M yds. Once a modest exporter of
cotton in the early 1960s, Burma has experienced historically declining
production and rising demand. As a consequence, while unsuccessfully trying
to stimulate production, it has been forced to import cotton yarns and lint
cotton, as well as cotton and synthetic fabrics. Domestic requirements have
increased from about 12,300 to 21,830 tons of lint cotton over the 1974-78
period, or by 15.4% p.a. As a result, Burma moved from self-reliance in raw
fibers to importing 8,025 tons, about 37% of needs, by 1978. Incremental
seed cotton produced under the project would total about 42,100 tons p.a. at
full development. GOB-purchased quantities, expected to reach about 86% of
this increment, would permit the substitution of about 12,100 tons of domestic
lint for an equivalent level of imports. Additionally, derived by-products
of oilcake, about 10,200 tons, would increase current exports (CY77: 72j400
tons); and extracted oil, about 4,000 tons, would allow reduction in imports
of substitute industrial oils (CY77: 10,000 tons).
8.21      Residual Crops.  A variety of traditional cash crops would be grown
on the remainder of cropped area under the project. Unlike with assigned
crops such as paddy and cotton, the individual grower has considerably more
flexibility about which of these residual crop alternatives to cultivate and
how to market output. For model purposes, the representative cropping patterns
in Annex 1 Table 8 would provide incremental residual production as follows:
(a) onions, 29,100 tons; (b) sesame, 6,700 tons; (c) groundnuts, 9,800 tons;
(d) chillies, 5,800 tons; and (e) pulses, 400 tons. In fact, a much wider
assortment would be grown, including peas, beans, other vegetables, wheat,
and maize. Incremental output of these commodities can easily be absorbed
through increased farm family consumption as well as in local and domestic



- 52 -
urban markets. Surplus onions, chillies, and vegetable oils from the project
area are traditionally shipped to Mandalay and Rangoon. With anticipated
growth of population (2.2% p.a.) and increase in real income (2.0-2.5% p.a.)
there would be an assured market for residual output. Should temporary,
localized gluts arise, thereby depressing farmgate prices, farmers would have
sufficient alternatives to maintain estimated farm income (paras 8.22-8.27).
Farm Income
8.22      The impact of the project on farm and family incomes was analyzed
for three different farm-size models in each irrigation-agronomic zone bene-
fiting from the project: specifically, paddyland and ya land in both the
currently irrigated tract and in the currently dryland farming zone. Because
of the relatively high productivity in the area already irrigated, farm sizes
there tend to be smaller and regional population densities greater (see
Annex 1, Table 1). For this area, therefore, representative farm sizes of 3 ac,
5 ac, and 12 ac were used. Alternatively, for the newly irrigated tract where
holdings tend to be more extensive, model farm sizes of 5 ac, 12 ac, and 27 ac
were chosen. Farm size-specific cropping patterns were developed, but for input
requirements and yields, constant returns to scale were assumed. Net farm
income levels for model farms, therefore, were considered a function of respec-
tive cropping intensity, extent of cultivation of each crop, and on-farm labor
self-sufficiency. Hired labor requirements have been estimated assuming monthly
family labor availability of 57 mandays 1/. Net farm income estimates, there-
fore, include remuneration to family labor and management. Estimates were
made for non-farm income, assuming that farm family labor can find additional
employment for 20% of its free time and can earn approximately the same wages
paid to hired workers 2/. Annex 3 Tables 8-11 present estimates of net farm
income and family income. Annex 3 Table 7 provides a summary of such esti-
mates and income per capita for each farm model. The latter table also pro-
vides information concerning the number and aggregate cultivated area of.
farms in each zone represented by the respective model farm. The following
table summarizes present and projected net farm incomes within each project
agronomic zone:
1/   Assumes an average 6-member family with 2.6 man-equivalent workers avail-
able for labor.
2/   In consonance with FAO/CP field observations in the project area.

8.23      A number of factors explain the large disparity in relative incre-
mental benefits from the project as well as expected absolute income disparity
within the project area. Firstly, farmers in the paddy area of the currently
irrigated tract already enjoy relatively high cropping intensity and high net
farm income relative to their dryland counterparts. These farmers would real-
ize a relatively modest increase in cropping intensity with higher output from
crops already cultivated. In contrast, farmers located in dry areas currently
contend with low intensities, low productivity (with high risk), and resulting
low income; with the project, these farmers would more than double cropping
intensities and adopt more productive irrigated crop activities. Therefore,
part of the relative divergence in project-related increases in net farm
incomes can be viewed as a catching up on the part of dry tract farmers to
income levels already reached in the currently irrigated area.
8.24      Secondly, the summary table above demonstrates the relatively large
projected difference in net farm income for paddy and cotton growers. In



- 54 -
computing farm incomes, compulsory paddy quotas were valued at the controlled
price and the balance of paddy production was valued at the local open market
price. The relatively low GOB-administered paddy price level and the progres-
siveness of the system depress the value of farm output (Annex 3, Table 1 and
paras 8.09-8.12). Even assuming a drop in the free cotton market price with
the project (Annex 3, Table 19), cotton proves more profitable than paddy.
Assuming with-project yields, input requirements, and quotas, monsoon HYV
paddy net returns total about K 320/ac, whereas medium-staple cotton provides
about K 1,800/ac (assuming total cotton output valued at procurement price
levels). If all paddy were assessed at the open market price, net returns per
ac of paddy would increase to about K 800/ac, equalling only about 44% of that
achievable with cotton on well-drained ya land. Pricing all of the representa-
tive 5 ac farmer's paddy at free market levels would boost net farm income to
about K 9,240, or about 26% above that projected with the project and with the
quota; however, the adjusted net income estimate would still be about 30%
below that anticipated for the 5 ac model cotton cultivator.
8.25      Thirdly, the cotton grower would benefit not only from high returns
per ac of cotton relative to paddy, but, as may be observed from projected
cropping patterns (paras 8.02-8.03), would have greater scope to grow comple-
mentary cash crops (chillies, onions, and sesame).
8.26      While recognizing that paddy growers' income growth would be rela-
tively constrained by low procurement prices, the progressiveness of quotas,
and relatively limited capability to augment income with lucrative cash crops,
it is felt that these farmers would still have incentives to participate in
the project. Even paddy growers in the currently irrigated tract would enjoy
14-36% boosts in family income (which adjusts for incremental non-farm income
losses with the project). As Annex 3, Table 7 shows, even the 3 ac farmer in
the currently irrigated paddyland area would achieve per capita income with
project above the IBRD-estimated national average of US$120. 1/ Farmers in
this area would earn about K 16 per incremental manday of family labor from
crop activities with the project and an estimated K 15 of family income per
incremental family manday devoted to project activities. This added income,
which is considerably above the periodically available off-farm wage of about
K 7/manday, should therefore provide incentive to participate in the project.
However, cotton growers, particularly those having above 5 ac, are anticipated
to derive family incomes well above the estimated national average. For
example, the 12 ac cotton farmer is projected to realize per capita family
income of about US$700 thus placing his family in the current top one percent
of the estimated national income distribution.
8.27      To rectify these significant income imbalances between paddy and
cotton growers, as well as those anticipated between project beneficiaries
and individuals outside the project area, it is expected that farm sizes
within the project area would be assessed and adjusted particularly in the
currently dry land scheduled to receive irrigation with the project. The
division of existing farms larger than 5 ac scheduled to adopt the proposed
cotton rotation and of those larger than 12 ac scheduled to adopt the paddy
1/   GNP adjusted for direct and indirect business taxes and corporate savings.



- 55 -
is expected to be considered. Such land redistribution can be effected, in
accordance with standard GOB practice, by Village Land Committees through
powers conferred under the Land Nationalization Act of 1953 and Tenancy Act
of 1964. Each township currently maintains a list of families with insuffi-
cient land and landless families to whom such redistributable land could be
allocated. It is recommended that GOB distribute land eligible for realloca-
tion according to the following priorities: (a) any remaining families in
need of resettlement from the encatchment and auxiliary areas of the dam;
(b) farmers whose land is to be totally or partially occupied by project
irrigation works (the number of such families and the amount of land needed
would be determined after ID finalizes the alignments of embankments, drains,
or other works); (c) families who have not been allocated land or who would
have sub-marginal holdings with full project implementation; and (d) state
farms and cooperatives.
Agricultural Cost Recovery
8.28      Cost recovery aspects of the agricultural component (Annex 3,
Table 14) were analyzed from two perspectives. 1/ Under Case I, it was
assumed that GOB would adjust its controlled input and output prices over time
to maintain relative prices within the economy. This would entail increasing
nominal prices by the domestic rate of inflation projected to rise on average
by 7.5% p.a., 1980-90. Alternatively, under Case II, it was assumed that
given GOB's adversion historically to adjust nominal prices on inflationary
grounds, the present regime of controlled prices would remain unchanged over
the 1980-90 period (paras 1.07-1.08).
8.29      Capital and Recurrent Costs and Subsidies.  The capital costs of
project works--dam, irrigation, drainage, flood control, agricultural support,
land clearance, and O&M during implementation--would amount to about K 663 M
in present value (PV) terms. 2/ Recurrent expenses for project works O&M
would amount to about K 48 M TPV). Based on projected international prices
and GOB-controlled prices, subsidies on incremental fertilizers, pesticides,
and tractor services (paras 8.10-8.12 and Annex 3, Table 14) required for the
project would total (PV) an estimated K 216 M in Case I and K 328 in Case II.
Thus, the PV of total capital and recurrent project costs and subsidies over
the 50-year projected life of the project would equal about K 932 M (Case I)
and K 1,045 M (Case II).
8.30      Recoveries through Land Taxation.  Farmers are now obliged to pay
a fixed land tax that varies by location according to land quality and avail-
able irrigation/drainage infrastructure. Rates paid are modest, ranging
from about K 1-2 per ac in dryland areas to about K 5-7 per ac in irrigated
1/   Analyses were done in constant 1980 currency units.
2/   Eight percent p.a. discount rate assumed to represent the financial
opportunity cost of capital for GOB. US$ converted to kyats at the
official rate of K 6.7/US$.  Includes physical contingencies but
excludes price contingencies.



- 56 -
zones. Anticipating standard tax reassessments by SLRD in the project area,
incremental tax revenues were conservatively estimated (Annex 3, Table 14)
at about K 0.7 M p.a. in nominal terms beginning with full development; after
deflation, these were charged toward project cost recovery. On a PV basis,
such incremental revenues over the project life would total about K 1.7 M.
8.31      Recoveries through GOB Compulsory Paddy Procurement.  The present
administration of the procurement system would tax production in the project
area directly and progressively, and incremental collections would represent
a means of project cost recovery. In theory, the system would take about 75-
80% of the paddy production increment. In practice, the amount would be
considerably less, probably about 60%, reflecting the fact that crop assessors
consistently underestimate crop yields to avoid farmer dissent. The difference
between the 1990 projected rice export price and GOB's procurement price plus
milling, handling, storage, and transport costs is a tax in paddy equivalents
amounting to US$140 (pre-monsoon) and US$131 (monsoon) per ton (Case I) 1/
(Annex 3, Table 3). Thus, the increase in GOB net procurement earnings would
amount to about K 65 M (Case I) or K 83 M (Case II) p.a. from incremental paddy
procurement (49,800 tons rice) at full development. The PV of the stream of
such implicit tax over the project life would total about K 454 M (Case I) or
K 556 M (Case II) (Annex 3, Table 13).
8.32      Recoveries through GOB Compulsory Cotton Purchases.  Incremental
cotton produced under the project would serve as an import substitute, and
cotton procured through GOB's compulsory purchase system would be a source of
cost recovery for the project. Similarly, cottonseed oil derived from the
incremental compulsory purchases would substitute for imported industrial oils
and cottonseed cake obtained could increase current exports of oilseed cake;
the latter would therefore provide two more, but relatively minor, sources of
cost recovery. While GOB purchasing centers represent the only legal selling
channel for medium-staple cotton, GOB cotton purchases have been considerably
below production levels to date (para 8.17). However, with the strengthening
of GOB's purchasing infrastructure and the geographical concentration of pro-
ject cotton production, GOB is expected to purchase at least 86% of incremental
medium-staple production derived from the project. The difference between the
1990 projected border-based seed cotton price and GOB's purchasing price for
seed cotton plus ginning, handling, storage, and transport adjustments is a tax
amounting to an estimated US$285 (medium-staple) and US$288 (short-staple) per
ton of seed cotton (Case I) 2/ (Annex 3, Tables 4 and 5). Thus, the increase
in GOB's net procurement earnings would amount to about K 69 M p.a. (Case I)
or K 144 M p.a. (Case II) from incremental purchases (36,200 tons) at full
development. The PV of such implicit tax over the project life would total
about K 482 M (Case I) or K 995 M (Case II) (Annex 3, Table 13).
1/   Case II equivalents:  US$171 (pre-monsoon) and US$162 (monsoon).
2/   Case II equivalents:  US$592 (medium-staple) and US$508 (short-staple).

Under both cases, full incremental costs would be adequately covered princi-

fall between these two bounds, implying that the cost recovery ratio would
exceed 100%. This analysis demonstrates, moreover, that were GOB to increase
controlled output prices, these measures would initially have a negative
effect on the budget. However, were production sufficiently price-elastic,
procurement and exports (import substitution) could rise to more than offset
initial losses.
8.34      Assurances were obtained from GOB that it would provide periodic
review and, if necessary, revision of its paddy and seed cotton pricing systems
to guarantee that farmers in the project area have adequate financial incentive
to make the investments necessary to increase production; and in the event that
the existing compulsory paddy procurement system and/or compulsory cotton sales
program are (is) modified or abolished, GOB would establish and maintain
arrangements in consultation with IDA for the recovery of the capital and
O&M costs for the agricultural component of the project.
Financial Aspects of Power
8.35      To be able to achieve reasonable cost recovery through EPC's tariff
for the capital investments in the power component, these investments 2/
should be made part of EPC's own assets. This assumes that EPC should in due
course be allowed to set tariffs so as to cover all operating expenses and to
earn a reasonable return on its rate base. Assurances therefore, were obtained
from GOB that the investments in the power component would be owned by EPC.
1/   With existing procurement price regimes, this estimate is considered
a lower bound since reduced GOB rice shipments from Lower Burma greater
than incremental quotas collected in the project area would also serve
for cost recovery. A similar case applies for cotton if GOB-controlled
imports fall as a result of goods manufacture and supply from seed cotton
withheld from TexCorp buying depots.
2/   As defined in Annex 2(b), Table 1.



- 58 -
8.36      Electricity Tariff.  Present tariffs, which average about 23 pyas
(US43.4) per kWh, have been in effect since October 1, 1976. Three different
tariff schedules are applied by geographical area: (a) Rangoon; (b) Pegu,
Tharrawaddy, Prome, and Loikaw; and (c) other areas, The tariff structure is
based on the block system, with kWh charges declining with increasing use.
Even for industrial consumers, a demand charge system does not exist. Present
tariff schedules are shown in Annex 3, Table 15. While general purpose rates
average a reasonable 42.5 pyas (USe 6.3) per kWh, rates for industrial use are
low, averaging 12.8 pyas (US4 1.9) per kWh. The share of industrial sales in
total sales is about 57%, but due to the low industrial rate, the industrial
revenue share is only 32%.
8.37      A modest tariff increase of about 14.5%, to be accomplished by
reducing the number of blocks and thereby primarily increasing industrial
rates, was proposed by EPC to MI-2 in February 1979. Assurances were obtained
from GOB that the proposed tariff increase of about 14.5% would be implemented
during FY 1980/81. Based on EPC's forecast of its financial position, it
appears that for EPC to achieve reasonable operating results, tariffs should be
further increased. Assurances, therefore, were obtained from GOB that:
(a)  a comprehensive study of the electricity tariff structure
and levels would be carried out for the purpose of improving
financial performance of EPC through tariff modifications;
and
(b) the results of the study and GOB's proposals on tariff mod-
ifications would be submitted to IDA by March 1981.
8.38      Fiscal Rate of Return (FRR) to the Power Component. 1/  EPC/GOB
would realize a 6.9% FRR 2/ based on the new tariff rates (averaging 26.5
pyas or US4 4/kWh) and future revisions to keep electricity tariffs relative
to other prices in the economy. Alternatively, should the new rates remain
unchanged over the 1980-90 period (para 8.28), EPC/GOB would achieve a FRR of
0.6% (Annex 3, Table 17). These results clearly demonstrate the need for GOB
to review its tariff structure and level for maintenance and enhancement of
real revenues. With this view and in the context of total EPC operations, as
specified above, agreement will be sought with GOB to study the electricity
tariff structure and level with a view to adjust rates to permit EPC to oper-
ate with greater financial integrity, in consonance with stated GOB objectives.
1/   FRR excludes taxes and duties.
2/   In constant 1980 currency units.
