IX. BENEFITS, JUSTIFICATION, AND RISKS
Overall Effects
9.01      Agriculture.  The project would principally enable intensification
and upgrading of agriculture in the project area through rehabilitation of an
existing irrigation network, expansion of the irrigation system into a cur-
rently dry area, and installation of complementary infrastructure such as
extension, agricultural research, and implement servicing. Through annual
incremental paddy production of 121,000 tons, the project would assist Burma
in recapturing part of its once large export market; the project would permit
rice exports at full development (1994) to expand by about 49,800 tons p.a.,
or about 6% above projected 1983 levels (900,000 tons). The project area
alone would be able to produce an estimated 42,100 incremental tons of seed
cotton at full development, an amount equal to 1978 estimated production
nationwide. In addition, cotton output from the project would be totally
medium-staple cotton, much in demand for Burma's milling industry. The
project would thus allow Burma to conserve scarce foreign exchange by substi-
tuting incremental domestic cotton production (12,100 tons of lint) for an
equivalent amount of imported intermediate and finished textile commodities.
Byproducts from GOB-procured incremental seed cotton are expected to reach
annually about 10,200 tons of oilseed cake (exportable commodity) and 4,000
tons industrial oil (import substitute). The project would also enable
greater farm family use and off-farm sales.
9.02      Power.  The project would also provide the economy with 28 MW firm
capacity 1/ of electric power with an average annual energy production of 165
GWh. With projected future demand (paras 3.08-3.11), installation of the
Nyaunggyat hydroelectric power facility would permit Burma to forego a rela-
tively costly petroleum-fueled thermal alternative to generate equivalent
power (para 9.07).
Employment Opportunities in Agriculture
9.03      Through the project, farm labor requirements in the project area
would increase from about 8.3 M mandays at present to an estimated 22.3 M man-
days p.a. at full development (year 14). With increased land productivity,
on-farm utilization of family labor would practically double, principally
through increased work activities in currently slack periods. At the present
time, laborers are hired for about 1.0 M mandays each year, or about 12% of
requirements; with the project, hired labor requirements would rise to about
8.4 M1 mandays, or about 38% of requirements. Landless families and farm
families allocated sub-economic holdings are now the principal suppliers of
such labor; additionally, dryland farmers frequently migrate into the currently
irrigated tract to assist in paddy transplanting and harvesting. However,
given higher land use intensities anticipated with the project, overall scope
for inter-tract labor migration may be somewhat curtailed. Additional require-
ments should be adequately met, however, through the projected 39% increase
1/   Guaranteed six hours peak output at 90% probability level.



- 60 -
(0.7 M mandays/mo) in the project area labor force by full development and
through seasonal labor obtained traditionally from neighboring Meiktila and
Myingyan Townships.
Household Income Effects from the Agricultural Component
9.04      As discussed in paras 8.22-8.27 and summarized in Annex 3, Table 7,
current family incomes in the project area mainly reflect present access to
irrigation facilities, or a lack thereof. Therefore, once infrastructure
and supporting services are installed throughout the area, farmers would
benefit by varying degrees because of differing agricultural circumstances
without the project. In the rehabilitation tract at the present time, few
farm families appear to be below the current IBRD-estimated absolute poverty
line of US$68 per capita p.a.; however, about 43% of these farm families lie
between this estimated poverty line and the national average per capita income
of US$120 p.a. In the dryland zone, on the other hand, most farmers appear
to fall below the absolute poverty line; and about 50% at or below the esti-
mated relative poverty line of US$36 per capita p.a. Therefore, for the total
project area, an estimated 19% of area farm families currently earn per capita
incomes at or below the estimated relative poverty line; another 19%, between
the estimated relative and absolute poverty levels; 27%, between the absolute
poverty line and the national average; and about 35%, above the national
average. As presented in Table 7, minimal change is anticipated in the future
were the project not undertaken. However, with successful project implementa-
tion all farm families are likely to realize per capita income levels above
the national average.
9.05      The relatively conservative assumption that farms in the newly
irrigated area larger than 12 ac will be subdivided has been incorporated into
farm budget and economic analyses. This assumption would mean, for example,
that at least 3,900 additional families could be allocated irrigated plots.
This allocation would represent about 40% of the currently landless families
or 31% of the new families projected for the project area by full development.
It is hoped that further action along these lines can be accomplished on even
smaller farms expected to begin intensive cotton cultivation. In addition,
about 11,000 acres of bush land would be developed and irrigated through the
project. This area could accommodate at least 1,100 settler families--a group
well in excess of the 100 families to be displaced from the dam area
(para 7.29).
Hydroelectric Power
9.06      Power Output.  The proposed Nyaunggyat hydroelectric power plant
would provide 28 MW firm capacity at a probability level of 90% and an aver-
age annual energy of 165 GWh. The plant would provide power to the system
at variable rates according to daily power demand and irrigation water needs,
with power generation ranging from six hours per day at 33 MW in July to 13
hours per day at 52 MW in November on average. The amount of incremental
energy that could be absorbed into the existing transmission system during the
initial period after project completion has been calculated based on the demand
for the northern area (Mandalay, Chauk, and Thazi) and its load curve. About



- 61 -
128 GWh, 143 GWh, and 155 GWh could be absorbed in 1986, 1987, and 1988,
respectively. From 1989, the total output of 165 GWh would be utilized fully
in the northern area. Energy absorbed during the first three years can be
broken into two functional categories: (a) a portion to meet incremental
demand requirements; and (b) a portion to substitute for Kyunchaung gas
turbine generation (implying fuel savings). Subsequent to this first three-
year period, incremental energy would be allocated solely to satisfy incre-
mental demand.
9.07      Least Cost Alternative Analysis for Power.  In the absence of an
established system development plan for the power sector through which to
determine whether the Nyaunggyat power component would represent the least
cost approach to meeting anticipated power needs, a simplified project-based
approach was adopted for the analysis. Burma's energy resources include
hydro-potential and limited hydrocarbon fossil fuels. Since there is no other
hydroelectric project that is ready for implementation, the only realistic
alternative to c,ompare with the Nyaunggyat power plant would be a thermal
alternative using petroleum fuel. There are, however, a number of different
types of thermal plants which could be considered. A conventional steam
thermal plant was excluded since it would be less efficient than medium-speed
diesel in view of the plant's size and operating characteristics. Four alter-
natives to the Nyaunggyat plant were analyzed. Those examined include: an
all medium-speed diesel engine (Alternative I); an all gas turbine (Alterna-
tive II); a combination of medium-speed diesel engine (50%) and gas turbine
(50%) (Alternative III); and a combination of medium-speed diesel engine (70%)
and gas turbine (30%) (Alternative IV). These four alternatives--each provid-
ing equivalent firm output and annual energy as anticipated for Nyaunggyat--
were compared by calculating present values (PV) of cost streams over a range
of discount rates. Cost streams, including initial capital, replacement, O&M,
and fuel costs, were developed for the 50-year project life. All costs have
been expressed in economic prices by converting local costs to border kyats.
It was found that Alternative IV would be the least cost thermal alternative
at discount rates above 12% (Annex 3, Table 16).
9.08      The economic cost stream of the power component, consisting of incre-
mental capital, replacement, and O&M costs, was compared with that of Alter-
native IV, the least cost thermal alternative. It was found that the discount
rate which equates the PVs of the two streams would be 15.9%, implying that
the power component of the project would be the least cost solution at discount
rates below 15.9%.
Benefits to the Economy
9.09      Agriculture.  Based on projected 1990 international prices (in 1980
currency units), the annual gross foreign exchange value of incremental pro-
duction of tradeables generated through the project is estimated at about
US$71.4 M at full development. Net of foreign exchange costs for traded recur-
rent inputs (US$6.8 M) and for O&M costs of the dam, irrigation system, and
agricultural support (US$0.5 M), the remaining foreign exchange value would
total about US$64.1 M p.a. Considering all capital and recurrent costs and
benefits valued in border kyats, the Burmese economy is expected to realize
net benefits from agricultural investments of about K 774.4 M (US$115.6 M)



- 62 -
in PV terms, which when annualized over the 50-year expected project life,
would yield about K 93.3 M p.a. (US$13.9 M). For total agricultural capital
costs incurred of about K 4,040 (US$603) per ac affected, annual net benefits
derived at full development would average about K 1,700 (US$254) per ac.
9.10      Power.  By constructing the Nyaunggyat plant, Burma foregoes costs
associated with power generation by the next cheapest alternative (Alternative
IV; see Annex 3, Table 16). The PV of capital and recurrent costs associated
with this thermal plant and the natural gas savings from the Kyunchaung unit
total K 263.3 M (US$39.3 M). The PV of estimated incremental capital and re-
current costs incurred through the Nyaunggyat facility is K 207.9 (US$31.0 M).
Therefore, Burma would benefit from an estimated net cost savings (PV), of
about K 55.4 M (US$8.3 M).
Economic Analysis
9.11      The economy of Burma would realize an economic rate of return (ERR)
from total project investments, inclusive of physical contingencies, of 21.3%.
Were the power component of the project excluded, the PV 1/ of net benefits
from remaining project investments would drop from K 829.8 M to K 774.4 M.
Therefore, since the power component enhances viability, on economic grounds
the multipurpose nature of the project is sound. The analysis implies that
through implementation of the multipurpose project, lower economic costs would
be incurred to achieve anticipated output levels than were separate irrigation
and power projects undertaken. The economic cash flow for the total project
is provided in Annex 3, Table 19 with a full referencing of all underlying
assumptions. The following, however, outline the principal bases of the
economic analysis:
(a)  Project Life.  The project would have an anticipated life of
50 years. Project implementation would require six years:
(i) five for dam construction and installation of the power
plant; but (ii) six for completion of irrigation works.
Agricultural benefits would reach full development level 14
years after the start of the project, implying an eight-year
build-up period to achieve with-project yields once project
irrigation/rehabilitation is provided. Phasing of project
irrigation is specified in Annex 1, Table 11. The power
plant is expected to achieve full development levels by
project year 9, four years after power plant completion.
(b)  Prices.  For internationally traded goods, farmgate prices
were derived from projected 1990 world market prices expressed
in 1980 currency values. Appropriate adjustments were made
for freight, handling, processing, and quality differentials.
Local costs and locally valued benefits were expressed in
border kyats through application of appropriate conversion
factors, as specified in Annex 3, Tables 1-5 and 19. The
1/   12% discount rate.



- 63 -
principal factor used for this purpose was a Standard Conver-
sion Factor (SCF) of 0.80. 1/
(c) Benefits. Incremental crop benefits were based on most
probable yields attainable with project investments. Power
benefits were based on avoidance of costs associated with
thermal power.
(d) Crop Production Costs. Per ac input requirements assumed are
provided in Annex 1, Table 9. The total farm labor contingent,
including family inputs, was assessed at the estimated average
market rate expressed in border kyats (Annex 3, Table 1). Ad-
justment was made in the crop production cost stream to reflect
realistically the staggered incurrence of costs and generation
of benefits in agricultural activities (Annex 3, Table 19).
(e) Secondary and Other Minor Project Effects. The following
effects have been omitted from the economic analyses and are
considered largely offsetting: Benefits: (a) demonstration
effect to farmers outside the project area; (b) incremental
net benefits from project-financed T&V extension efforts in
project townships outside the project area; (c) incremental
employment generated for commodity processing, handling,
transport, and marketing; (d) likely build-up of exploitable
fish populations in the reservoir; (e) flood control; and
(f) remaining economic life and scrap value of construction
equipment. Costs: (a) poor quality timber and firewood scrub
sacrificed in the reservoir area; (b) agricultural output from
about 650 ac of land cultivated under rainfed, subsistence
conditions in two small villages within the reservoir area.
Project Risk
9.12      The Nyaunggyat project would represent Burma's first venture involv-
ing ICB for civil works construction. The risk level, from a potential bidder's
perspective, would be relatively high since there are some unknown factors
affecting contractors' working conditions in Burma. To minimize this risk
GOB has agreed to remove various constraints and provide normal operations for
foreign contractors for ICB works. GOB has also agreed to construct the access
road to the damsite by force account (para 5.11).
9.13      Given the complexity of the project and the difficulties associated
with interrelated works involving ID, EPC, and AC (also a new experience in
Burma), it would be unrealistic to expect problem-free implementation. An
1/   This, as well as other conversion factors, represents the product of pre-
liminary estimates of adjustments to accommodate for the current regime
of taxes, duties, and other trade restrictions in Burma. More detailed
calculations in the future are likely to produce a lower SCF which would
increase project net economic benefits.



- 64 -
inherent risk in any multipurpose project concerns coordination in imple-
menting the various components. With this in mind, GOB has agreed to appoint
as Director for the Nyaunggyat project an ID officer of more senior rank
than directors for other IDA-financed projects in Burma.  Also, project
organization has been designed to facilitate early resolution of implementa-
tion bottlenecks at the highest levels through the PIC (paras 7.02 and 7.10).
9.14      The project could face some risk from the usual delays in posting
additional professional and technical staff, particularly design and construc-
tion engineers. There is no current or foreseen shortage of new engineering
graduates. However, mobilization of experienced construction engineers
requires reshuffling of some ID engineers already in service and employment of
new engineers. ID is aware of the problem and is expected to make necessary
arrangements for staff mobilization by August 1980.
9.15      The project would permit considerable improvement in productivity
over yields obtained currently under rainfed conditions. A relatively lengthy
eight-year educative period has been assumed to reach full development yields
once irrigation is provided. These yields are considered realistic given:
(a) current research farm results (Annex 1, Table 12); (b) proposed extension
input; (c) proposed demonstrations in the currently dryland tract to intro-
duce intensive cotton cultivation under irrigated conditions before the start
of irrigation; and (d) the demonstration effect from currently irrigated
areas. Farmers from the dryland tract, which forms a crescent around the
rehabilitation area, currently work within the irrigated zone during peak
periods to supplement farm income. This factor would be of great importance
to speed transfer of proposed irrigation technology into the new area.
However, projected yields are predicated upon GOB provision of sufficient
supplies of yield-augmenting inputs, extension services, and credit, as well
as of incentive output pricing. Assurances will be sought to ensure their
provision (paras 5.23, 7.06, and 8.34).
9.16      Sensitivity Tests.  Sensitivity tests were performed to assess the
impact of variations in incremental costs and benefit streams on the project's
viability. Switching values (sv) 1/ for individual and total cost and benefit
streams are provided in Annex 3, Table 12 (agricultural component), Table 18
(power component), and Table 20 (total project). These tables also present
PVs for total cost and benefit streams with their breakdown into respective
categories. While an opportunity cost of capital of 12% has been assumed
throughout the analysis, SVs were also calculated at lower and higher dis-
count rates (10-16% p.a.). A plot of PVs for total project cost and benefit
streams against various discount rates is presented in Annex 4, Chart 8 (a);
a locus of all possible SVs for aggregate costs and benefits 2/ is provided
1/   Switching value is the percentage change that reduces the net PV to
zero at a specified discount rate, e.g., the opportunity cost of
capital.
2/   Discount rate of 12%.



- 65 -
in Chart 8 (b). The SVs demonstrate, and the figures illustrate, the large
amount of shock the project and its components can bear and still retain
economic viability.
9.17      As another demonstration of resilience, the project's implementation
period was lengthened by two years to test for the effects of risks discussed
in paras 9.12-9.14. With delay of benefits and some costs to reflect this
change, the net PV for the total project would fall from a base case of
K 829.8 M to K 607.7 M, and the ERR would decline from 21% to about 19%.
Since the power component would still make a K 26.1 M contribution to the
project's net PV, the multipurpose nature of the project should be retained
even with this two-year extension. Lastly, if cotton yields reach only 1,000
lbs per ac with the project, instead of the 1,500 lbs anticipated, the net PV
for the agricultural component would decline by 46% to K 417.6 M and the net
PV for the total project would decrease to K 473.2 M, and the ERR would drop
to about 18%.
X. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
10.01     Agreements have been reached with GOB that:
(a) GOB would make arrangements to provide adequate seasonal
and investment credit to project area farmers (para 5.23);
(b) consultants would be appointed to assist the various
implementing agencies, including consultants for detailed
engineering and design of the Paunglaung hydroelectric
project, on terms and conditions satisfactory to IDA
(para 5.27);
(c) GOB would prepare and submit to IDA a power system planning
and operations review by April 1981 (para 5.35);
(d) EPC would undertake the following actions prior to the
commissioning of the power plant: (i) integration of the
Kyunchaung gas turbine with the hydro-grid, (ii) installa-
tion of additional transformers at the Mandalay and Thazi
substations, (iii) upgrading the transmission voltage for
the Thazi-Toungoo line from the currently planned 132 kV to
230 kV, and (iv) installation of additional transformer
at the Toungoo substation (para 5.36);
(e) with regard to accounts and audits,
(i) separate accounts for each project component would
be maintained by the respective implementing agen-
cies, including EPC, and collated by the Project
Implementation Committee (para 6.14);



- 66 -
(ii) these accounts would be audited annually in accord-
ance with sound auditing principles consistently
applied by the Director General, Central Accounts
Office, acting on behalf of the Council of the People's
Inspectors of GOB (para 6.14); and
(iii) the Project Implementation Committee would send to
IDA copies of the summary of an unaudited annual
project account within four months of the end of the
fiscal year, to be followed by certified copies of the
audited account and auditor's report within nine months
of the end of each fiscal year (para 6.14);
(f)  with regard to agricultural extension and input supplies,
(i)  the organizational set-up and work program for the
agricultural extension services for the project would
be satisfactory to IDA (para 7.06);
(ii) fertilizers would be supplied for all paddy and cotton
varieties, and for all crops in the project area at
rates to be recommended each year by the extension
service (para 7.06); and
(iii) each AC Township Supplies and Service Office would stock
sufficient sprayers and pesticides in the project area
on the basis of estimates to be made annually by the
extension service (para 7.06);
(g)  required additional professional staff for ID, AC and EPC for
project implementation would be approved and appointed not
later than October 31, 1980 in accordance with the agreed
schedule with IDA (para 7.13);
(h)  by October 31 of each year, IDA would receive for comment
the detailed project work program for the following calendar
year (para 7.22);
(i)  quarterly progress reports would be submitted to IDA within
one month of the end of the quarter under review, and annual
progress reports would be sent to IDA by March 31 of the
following year (para 7.23);
(j)  the Project Implementation Committee would establish a moni-
toring unit to conduct benchmark surveys every two years
beginning January 1981, covering, inter alia, cropped areas,
yields, use of farm inputs, and project-related changes in
area employment opportunities and in farm and family incomes
(para 7.24);



- 67 -
(k)  GOB would maintain records and procedures adequate to record
and monitor the progress of the project (including costs and
benefits), and furnish to IDA at regular intervals all such
information as IDA shall reasonably request concerning the
project, its cost, and benefits derived (para 7.24);
(1)  detailed regulations for reservoir operation would be sub-
mitted to IDA by September 1983 for review and comment
(para 7.27);
(m)  with regard to operation and maintenance of project facilities,
(i) the dam, allied structures, powerhouse, transmission
line, and all irrigation facilities, roads, and build-
ings constructed and rehabilitated under the project
would be maintained in accordance with sound engineer-
ing practices by the respective agencies including the
ID, AC, AMD, and EPC (para 7.27);
(ii) adequate staff and funds would be provided for this
purpose (para 7.27);
(iii) annual budgetary allocations for the various implementing
agencies would be appropriately increased to provide for
project-related recurrent expenditures (para 7.27); and
(iv) EPC would perform its operations, plan future develop-
ment of its power system, and maintain its financial
position in accordance with sound engineering, financial,
and administrative practices under the supervision of
experienced and competent management, and EPC would
furnish to IDA unaudited financial statements for its
operation within six months of the end of each fiscal
year to be followed by certified copies of its financial
statements and audit reports within nine months of the
end of each fiscal year (para 7.27);
(n)  the members of the Advisory Panel of Experts for dam safety
would be appointed by GOB on terms and conditions satisfactory
to IDA, prior to the award of the ICB civil works contract
(para 7.28);
(o)  a resettlement program including adequate compensation, would
be carried out for persons affected by dam construction in
accordance with a resettlement plan acceptable to IDA (para 7.29);
(p)  GOB would provide periodic review and, if necessary, revi-
sion of its paddy and seed cotton pricing systems to guaran-
tee that farmers in the p.roject area have adequate financial
incentive to make the investments necessary to increase
production; in the event that the existing compulsory paddy
procurement system and/or compulsory cotton sales program



- 68 -
are (is) modified or abolished, GOB would establish and
maintain arrangements in consultation with IDA for the
recovery of the capital and O&M costs for the agricultural
component of the project (para 8.34); and
(q)  the investments in the power component would be owned by
EPC (para 8.35);
(r)  with regard to the electricity tariff,
(i)  the proposed tariff increase of about 14.5% would
be implemented during FY 1980/81 (para 8.37);
(ii)  a comprehensive study of the electricity tariff
structure and levels would be carried out for the
purpose of improving financial performance of EPC
through tariff modifications (para 8.37); and
(iii)  the results of the study and GOB's proposals on
tariff modifications would be submitted to IDA
by March 1981
(para 8.37).
10.02     A condition of effectiveness for the IDA Credit would be the satis-
factory arrangements for commitments from co-financiers participating in
project financing (para 6.05).
10.03     With the above agreements and condition of credit effectiveness, the
proposed project is suitable for an IDA credit of US$90.0 M on standard IDA
terms. The Borrower would be the Socialist Republic of the Union of Burma.

7/ Represents a fuel savings for the Kyunchaung gas turbine. The economic cost
savings was estimated by equating the cost of heat content of natural gas
to that of heavy residual oil currently exported. The economic benefit
(cost saving) so derived represents about US$3.4/kWh.
